4.3 Development, Growth and the Environment

[In distancing itself from nature, [industrial masculinity]{.mark} is
[constructed through accomplishments based on]{.mark} positive
[masculine features such as **power, strength and
intelligence**]{.mark}.]{.underline} If [hard industries are dying,
these accomplishments must come from]{.underline} the development of
[other [traditionally **masculine interests**, such as
technology]{.underline}]{.mark}[.]{.underline} But two types of
masculinity are constructed on the basis of technological development.
Demonstrating that technology is fundamental to masculinity, it is also
key to eco-modern masculinity[,]{.underline} which developed out of
1970's ecological masculinity as opposition to industrial masculinity
(Hultman 2016:5). It "demonstrated caring and responsibility for the
environment while, at the same time promoting economic growth and
technological expansion" (Anshelm & Hultman 2014:92). [[Promoting
economic growth and technological expansion is]{.mark} as we shall see
[fundamental to]{.mark} the renewed **[industrial
masculinity]{.mark}**]{.underline}. But its lack of demonstrated caring
and responsibility for the environment is what separates it from
eco-modern masculinity. Regardless, [[technological development and
growth is key, so much so that it sees it as the way to build of the
future]{.underline}]{.mark} (GOP 2016:5). Republicans easily conflate
technological development with environmental conservation, but never
explicitly. It sees that "agriculture is directly impacted by the
constant advance in technology" (ibid.:18) having half a page before
announced that "Farmers and ranchers ... among this country's most
leading conservationists." (ibid.)

It is interesting to note that Republicans seem to suggest there is a
space for government interference to advance the protection of nature
through technological development:

> Modern farm practices and technologies, supported by programs from the
> Department of Agriculture, have led to reduced erosion, improved water
> and air quality, increased wildlife habitat, all the while maintaining
> improved agricultural yields (ibid.)

Noteworthy here is that the advancement of [technology is mainly in
focus. Environmental protection is simply a fortunate by-product of the
important agricultural yields]{.underline}. Large-scale technological
achievement is a recurring theme in the Platform. It seems to be the
solution to renewable energy: "Our nuclear industry, cleanly generating
almost 20 percent of our electricity from its 99 plants, has a
remarkable safety record." (ibid.:19)

The emphasis on energy as being clean would have been more significant
in this context had not coal also been referred to as such on the same
page. Clean seems to be a word used for appearance rather than
substance. [But [technology is opposed to nature]{.mark} in more subtle
ways]{.underline}. The Platform also suggests that "\[p\]ermitting for a
safe, non-polluting hydroelectric facility ... can take many years
because of the current President's hostility to dams" (ibid.). Here the
GOP invokes safety and nonpollution as central to their own concerns and
relates it to technological achievement. But it is a false image. By
reiterating the importance of allowing "responsible development of
nuclear energy" (ibid:20) the GOP seemingly ignores the very possible
threat that nuclear power also poses to nature. But technological
development requires investment. Which is why it is interesting to see
Republicans suggesting that "Poverty, not wealth, is the gravest threat
to the environment, while steady economic growth brings the
technological advances which make environmental progress possible."
(ibid.:21).

This demonstrates the artfulness of the Platform in weaving together
economic and technological development and growth seamlessly. By
relating environmental protection to technological development through
growth, Republicans suggest [[growth can only come if environmental
protection is less stringent, which enables]{.mark} more [technological
development. It holds nature as a **symbolic hostage** to economic
progress]{.mark} and future technology.]{.underline}

Opposing nature and government to people and economic growth is the most
frequent theme in the Platform. Republicans concern for Keystone XL
Pipeline clearly demonstrates this. The previous administration's
blocking of the pipe carrying oil from Canada to the U.S has according
to Republicans "\[...\] become a symbol in the contest between the
public's desire for economic development and the government's hostility
to growth." (ibid.:50)

It is suggested that the government is opposed to the people as well as
economic growth and prosperity. Yet it was the people protesting that
led the U.S state department to halt it due to environmental concerns
(BBC, 2017).

Whether it is the protection of nature or animals, the view of the
environment as opposed to growth and technological development keeps
reappearing. The Endangered Species Act (ESA) is seen as having stunted
economic development, halted the construction of projects, burdened
landowners \[....\] (GOP 2016:22). The protection of endangered species
is not a worthwhile goal on its own, if it cannot be financially
motivated: The ESA should ensure that the listing of endangered species
and the designation of critical habitats are based upon sound science
and balance the protection of endangered species with the costs of
compliance and the rights of property owners. (ibid.) The view of
natural resources as predominantly a source of income is perhaps most
clearly evidenced in the beginning of the Agriculture, Energy and
Environment chapter: We are the party of America's growers, producers,
farmers, ranchers, foresters, miners, commercial fishermen, and all
those who bring from the earth the crops, minerals, energy and the
bounties of our seas that are the lifeblood of our economy. (emphasis
added) (ibid:17) Bounty may mean either bonus or monetary reward, and
resources are the lifeblood of many economies. Yet, it is another piece
of the puzzle demonstrating how emphasising growth commodifies nature.
This view is not limited to Republicans but is strongly advocated by
them and as such strengthens a narrative wherein "\[t\]he idea of
unlimited growth \[...\] is based on a lie that there is an infinite
supply of the earths goods, and this leads to the planet being squeezed
dry at every limit." (Godfrey in Godfrey & Torres 2016:49). The
preoccupancy with economic progress returns in Trump's budget proposal.
Government agencies, particularly the EPA, face deep spending cuts to
incentivise economic growth by redirecting money to infrastructure and
the military (The New York Times, 2017; Fox News, 2017). The
preoccupancy of [[industrial masculinity with development through
economy and technology]{.underline}]{.mark} is almost eco-modern in its
promotion of technology as key to environmental protection. However, it
fails in that [it [commodifies nature, making the environment a source
of enrichment, rather than our shared home]{.mark} by taking into
consideration the economy rather than the well-being of
nature]{.underline}, first: We firmly believe environmental problems are
best solved by giving incentives for human ingenuity and the development
of new technologies, not through top-down, command-and-control
regulations that stifle economic growth and cost thousands of jobs. (GOP
2016:22)

There is no place in conservative discourse for seeing [[nature]{.mark}
as our whole world and home]{.underline}. Instead[, [it is an obstacle
to growth]{.mark} and development]{.underline}, the answer to our
problems [[according to industrial masculinity]{.underline}]{.mark}.
Technological advancement may seem like a move towards environmental
protection and is a promising turn away from fossil fuels. But
technological development of safe renewable sources is practically
omitted from the text. Wind and solar is only mentioned briefly. With
emphasis on cost-effectiveness and private capital, Republicans seem
concerned with money rather than renewable energy as they "\[...\]
encourage the cost-effective development of renewable energy sources --
wind, solar, biomass, biofuel, geothermal, and tidal energy -- by
private capital." (ibid.:20) The phrasing suggests a desire to remain
free from state interference. This would make it harder on new
investments in renewable energy since hard industries are already
economically and politically established and profitable. It also
suggests that renewable energy does not fit in with the image of
large-scale technological developments that showcase intelligence,
strength, hard work and efficiency, fundamental to the society
industrial masculinity built (Winter 2010: Appendix Table 6).
Prioritizing large-scale engineering is the most visible way in which
the [GOP [Platform clearly deprioritizes nature as
femininity]{.mark},]{.underline} in favour of technology and progress of
human civilization. Another example of this is the suggestion that the
U.S "phase out the federal transit program and reform provisions of the
Environmental Policy Act which can delay and drive up costs for
transportation projects" (GOP 2016:5). Republicans seek to cut funding
for environmentally friendly transportation methods by suggesting that
"\[e\]veryone agrees on the need for clean water and safe roads, rails,
bridges, ports and airports." (ibid:4). This is in keeping with Trump's
newly proposed budget with increases in infrastructure spending, offset
by cuts in environmental protection. This is interesting because it has
a gendered impact on the gap in American public transport. Women use
public transport far more, and more often, than men, despite often being
afraid to do so. It is often a forced choice driven by unequal
employment opportunities resulting in women often being trapped in
lowpaying jobs that make car ownership difficult, or traditional gender
roles leading to the man utilizing the car, or a concern for the
environment (CityLab, 2015; Bell 2016:5). But progress is not only a
positive value in industrial masculinity nor in conservativism.

When it concerns the private life such as family and social values,
tradition is critical. By tradition, nature is god-given and "...the
Republican Party reaffirms the moral obligation to be good stewards of
the God-given natural beauty and resources of our country." (GOP
2016:20). Nature as god-given seems to be the same view it has of
marriage, as "between one man and one woman, is the foundation for a
free society and has for millennia been entrusted with rearing children
and instilling cultural values" (ibid.:11). The gender role hypothesis
of marriage suggests women have historically been unequal in traditional
marriage as wife and mother compared to their male counterpart
(Strohschein, 2016). If true in the U.S as in India, this traditional
institution upon which culture is based, reproduces a fundamentally
unequal society in which men are owners and stewards of both women and
nature. The devaluation of femininity then inherent to traditional
marriage (Strohschein & Ram, 2016) can only mean that the institution
sustains and reproduces hierarchical supremacy of industrial
masculinity. [[Industrial masculinity need **modernized technology** and
traditional values to retain power and ownership over femininity as
nature.]{.underline}]{.mark}

### 2NC\-\--L\-\--Fossil Fuel Extraction

#### Fossil fuel extraction relies on masculine concepts of the "hard" masculine figure of the oil worker.

**Kangasluoma '20** \[Sohvi Kangasluoma, 14 December 2020, Sohvi
Kangasluoma is a Postdoctoral researcher at the University of Helsinki.
Her research focuses on the entanglements of Arctic oil and gas with
gender, emotions and environment, which her PhD dissertation examined,
"Drilling for the future: Gendered justifications of the Arctic fossil
fuel industry",
https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/polar-record/article/abs/drilling-for-the-future-gendered-justifications-of-the-arctic-fossil-fuel-industry/03F4263D617BEB87704A30C3A18787A2\]
//wastella

[Comprehending [fossil fuel production requires accounting for its
multiple societal dimensions]{.mark}, such as gender, race and
class.]{.underline} The literature discussing gender within extractive
industries usually refers to women's roles; however, gender as an
analytical tool could and should be broadened to examine the
masculinities and femininities within the oil and gas industry as well
as other intersecting identities and structures. In this paper, the
concept of gender refers to socially constructed identities and
differences between the categories of men and women, which allude to the
characteristics assigned to the categories of masculine and feminine.
These categories are always culturally specific and change over time
(Connell & Pearse, Reference Connell and Pearse2014; Hoogensen Gjørv,
Reference Hoogensen Gjørv, Latola and Savela2017; Tickner, Reference
Tickner2001). Extractive industries have been argued to belong to a
culture of masculinity (Daggett, Reference Daggett2018; Kojola,
Reference Kojola2019; Miller, Reference Miller2004), and they have
historically been rather male dominated. Daggett (Reference Daggett2018)
has forcefully argued that there is a relationship between fossil fuels
and the white patriarchal order, proposing the concept of
petro-masculinity. Miller (Reference Miller2004) has examined the oil
industry in Alberta, Canada, and concluded three aspects constructing
masculinity within the industry: "[[everyday interactions]{.mark} which
[exclude women]{.mark}; [values and beliefs specific to the dominant
occupation of engineering]{.mark} which [reinforce gender
divisions]{.mark}; and [a consciousness derived from]{.mark} the
powerful [symbols of the frontier myth]{.mark} and the romanticized
cowboy hero"]{.underline} (Miller, Reference Miller2004, p.47). [[In the
Arctic, the history of exploration together with the idea of conquering
nature has been updated and transferred to the modern hydrocarbon
industry]{.mark}.]{.underline} [(Female) [gender is missing from the
energyscape]{.mark} in the Barents region]{.underline} (Lempinen,
Reference Lempinen2018). [The imagery and role models visible to the
public to affect perceptions of the industry]{.underline} (European
Institute for Gender Equity, 2016, p.6). [Nevertheless, [the dichotomy
between femininity and masculinity is no longer so simplistic]{.mark}
[within the modern hydrocarbon industry]{.mark} in Norway and Russia,
[or within the Arctic]{.mark}, and "the debate is already moving beyond
such stereotypes and structural limitations"]{.underline} (Vladimirova &
Habeck, Reference Vladimirova and Habeck2018, p.148).

In this article I focus on the justifications utilised to support the
continuing production as well as on the construction of gender. When
analysing the material, it became evident that especially in the Russian
context, the presentation of gender meant a presentation of masculinity,
as the majority of the people presented in the webpages were male.
Connell, a pioneer in masculinity studies, emphasises that we should
discuss masculinities, not masculinity. Multiple masculinities exist
within a certain gender order (Connell, Reference Connell1995) and the
performance on masculinity relies "on maneuvering between several
competing gender regimes which function within historical gender orders"
such as the Soviet and post-Soviet context (Yusupova, Reference
Yusupova2016, p.31). The construction and performance of various
masculinities can be only done within a certain framework laid out by
the state (Yusupova, Reference Yusupova2016). For instance, Vladimir
Putin's "hypermasculinity" is playing a crucial role in the contemporary
re-masculinisation of Russia (Sperling, Reference Sperling2015;
Yusupova, Reference Yusupova2016). Ventsel (Reference Ventsel2018) has
examined the dominant masculinities in Siberia, specifically in the
Republic of Sakha (Yakutia), where the discourse of conquering nature
has deep roots. [In Soviet times, Yakutia was one of the nation's
[resource-extraction frontiers, characterised by a pronounced]{.mark}
proletarian ["hard" masculinity]{.mark} that continued well into the
2000s.]{.underline} [[Recently]{.mark}, according to Ventsel, [more
urban, "softer" masculinities have emerged]{.mark} (Ventsel Reference
Ventsel2018, pp.210--211).]{.underline} [The [masculine identity of an

towards stricter definition of gender roles can be interpreted as a
result of colonial regimes and modernisation, also in the context of the
Russian (and Norwegian) north (Vladimirova & Habeck, Reference
Vladimirova and Habeck2018; Ziker, Reference Ziker2010), since many
indigenous cultures have traditionally not had binary gender division
and roles.

#### Masculinist technological determinism spurs a never-ending cycle of destruction\-\--disruptions and affordances are facilitated by new developments. 

By Gillian **Youngs, 24**; \"Feminist International Relations Through a
Technospatial Lens,\" Taylor & Francis, 11-13-2024,
https://www-taylorfrancis-cm.proxy.lib.umich.edu/books/mono/10.4324/9781032656427/feminist-international-relations-technospatial-lens-gillian-youngs.
//xav!

[[The impacts of]{.mark} [**masculinist technological determinism** have
grown in complexity as developments]{.mark} in industrial [economy sit
alongside those in digital economy]{.mark}, and [patterns of
transformation are shaped by linkages across them]{.mark}]{.underline}.
[[Evolutionary processes]{.mark} in industrial economy [have]{.mark}
increasingly [been impacted by]{.mark} the [disruptions and affordances
facilitated by **digital developments**]{.mark}]{.underline}.
All-encompassing characteristics of digital economy also raise new
stakes for thought about political economy and social change. Examples
of the breadth of digital impacts make this readily evident in areas as
diverse as: online activism linking global and local communities and the
exponential growth of web-enabled outreach and engagement involving
nongovernmental organizations (NGOs) and their supporters;
[[cyberattacks on businesses and state-based cyberwarfare and
cyberdefence]{.mark};]{.underline} multiplyingr forms of online
production consumption of goods and services; local and global networks
of professional, [[commercial and personal as well as criminal relations
and practices]{.underline}]{.mark}; virtual experience, play, leisure
and intimacy including gaming, entertainment and internet dating. Varied
and ever-changing forms of online presence, mobility and access are in
play throughout, whether concerning people or entities (for example
states, organizations and businesses), places, information or
opportunities to disrupt for positive or negative effects.
Transformations in reach and speed are core to understanding how digital
infrastructures, systems and activities have evolved, added to and
reshaped aspects of industrial society, as well as introducing
completely new possibilities which could not have been imagined in
earlier industrial times.

Contemporary modernity in its combination of industrial and digital
economy is a result of: the information and communication technologies
(ICTs) revolution through the latter half of the 20th into the 21st
centuries; the arrival of the personal computer and desktop computing;
the Internet and World Wide Web; and the growth of mobile telephony and
computing along with apps so that working, communicating, relating and
consuming on the move is an everyday reality. The [[driving momentum
through]{.mark} all these **[developments]{.mark}** has been [to make
instantaneity increasingly the norm]{.mark} whether in: [devastating
effects of **cyberattacks** on governments, businesses]{.mark} and
[organizations;]{.mark} [**proliferation of fake news** and its
distortions]{.mark} of offline processes; [**micro aggressions** of
cyberstalkers and internet trolling]{.mark}]{.underline}; or, on the
more positive side, global activism against ills such as slavery,
prejudice, political oppression and inequalities of all kinds; the
befriending and romantic possibilities of global gaming and internet
dating; as well as the familial, individual and collegial everyday
contact and intimacy of continuous communication. The embedding of ICTs
in these and other ways has introduced the need to think about economy
and society beyond the industrial model to incorporate fully virtual
phenomena and their implications.

### 2NC\-\--L\-\--Threat Construction

#### [Terrorist threat construction overtly excludes woman through masculinized narratives of protector and protected and further investment into UAV drone technology specifically puts increased burdens and risk of violence on women.]{.underline}

**Tinker & Sjoberg 11**(J. Ann Tickner distinguished scholar in
residence at the School of International Services, president of
the International Studies Association and Laura Sjoberg American

Homebase Editor for the International Feminist Journal of Politics

A [[feminist approach suggests]{.mark} two [additional]{.mark} lines of
[analysis of the war on terror within]{.mark}]{.underline}
IT/[IR]{.underline}: the ways in which the "unmanning" of combat
destabilizes already vexing discussions of women in combat, and its
possible [effects on [hierarchies of **Militarized Hegemonic
Masculinities.**]{.mark}]{.underline} []{.mark}IR feminists have
enriched our understandings of the events of September 11, 2001 and the
war on terror by, for example, highlighting the ways in which a
resurgence of [[masculinity found in]{.mark} the [symbolism
surrounding]{.mark} the events of [September 11]{.mark}, 2001 [denied
women]{.mark} a chance to [participate in]{.mark} the [heroic
confrontation]{.mark}]{.underline} with terrorism, analyzing the use of
women\'s rights as justificatory strategy for the campaign in
Afghanistan, and shedding light on the ways in which high-profile cases
such as those of Jessica Lynch, Shoshana Johnson, Cindy Sheehan, and
Lynndie England acted to gender representations of the war on terror
(Tickner 2002; Sjoberg 2007; Steans 2008). Feminist analysis helps us
understand how [[war on terror discourses rely]{.mark} up[on gender
stereotypes]{.mark}]{.underline} even as they offer problematic ideals
of the women soldier, unsettle our expectations about female violence,
and contribute to the evolution of constructions of militarized
femininity.

Further, IR feminists have shown how [[narratives about (male)
protector]{.mark} and ([female) protected]{.mark} [in]{.mark} the [war
on terror]{.mark} have been "[central]{.mark}]{.underline} to boundary
drawing processes since [[they construct]{.underline}]{.mark} a clear
[division between [the **'war front'**]{.mark}---[a masculinist
domain]{.mark}]{.underline} in which masculinity if affirmed in the
heroic actions performed on the battlefield---[and]{.mark} the [**'[home
front']{.mark}**[---a feminised realm]{.mark} of domesticity and
peace]{.underline}" (Steans 2008, 160). The [unmanning of combat has
complicated these boundary]{.underline} drawing practices greatly,
troubling assumptions about women in combat (questions about women\'s
capability in performing equal roles in combat), the nature of heroism,
as well as problematizing the battlespace/domestic space dichotomy.

Ostensibly, advances in technology should favor women who want to
participate in the armed services allowing them to bypass debates about
the lack of upper-body strength that detractors often claim is a prime
obstacle to their participation. The right to die in combat has
historically been denied to women, and as Tickner observes, "giving
one\'s life for one\'s country has been considered the highest form of
patriotism, but it is an act from which women have been virtually
excluded" (Tickner 1992, 28). Traditionally consigned to domestic
nurturing roles, women have recently gained entry to the military ranks,
only to find positions that may very well be automated in the near
future. In the meantime, however, it is much more difficult to argue
that women lack the inherent physical ability to operate the keyboards
and joysticks that control UAVs or other unmanned weapons. However, the
female tele-operators of unmanned technologies will likely have to guard
against a similar devaluing of their work as took place during their
prominence as computer programmers.

Similarly, the evolving norm of riskless warfare also raises questions
for women. Stiehm (1983) argues that the [[state]{.mark} typically
[denies women]{.mark} the [opportunity to be societal
"protectors]{.mark}," [assigning]{.mark} to [them]{.mark} the [role of
"protected]{.mark}"]{.underline} despite the predatory threat often
posed by their ostensible state guardians. If [military
service]{.underline} is tied to [women\'s status [as
citizen]{.mark}s]{.underline} and opportunities for political
participation, then what effect will the "unmanning" of combat have on
this dynamic? In other words, when soldiers are no longer asked to put
their lives at risk for the state, what becomes of women\'s struggle to
overcome second-class citizenship and solidify the gains seen in the
hardwon privilege of military service? Also, the expansion of unmanned
warfare further frustrates the development of what Judith Stiehm calls a
["defender" society]{.underline}, one "composed of citizens equally
liable to experience violence and equally responsible for exercising
society\'s violence" (1983, 367), [already limited [by a masculinist
state]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark}

[[Drone warfare]{.underline}]{.mark} represents a new stage in what Jean
Elshtain identified as the [American]{.mark} military [policy of
["combatant immunity,]{.underline}]{.mark}["]{.underline} the practice
of "riskless warfare" which relies upon aerial bombing campaigns that
[[punish]{.mark} foreign [noncombatants (especially women and
children]{.mark}]{.underline}) while prioritizing the avoidance of U.S.
casualties (Elshtain 2000, 447). Aside from the ethical issues this
practice raises, it is conceivable that the unmanning of combat will be
interpreted as American cowardice thus prompting perhaps a revision of
notions of heroism and an unsettling of certain American warrior
masculinities. Peter Singer of the Brookings Institute wonders whether
the message the American use of unmanned warfare is sending abroad may
be counterproductive, recounting the Lebanese editor who referred to
Israeli and American users of UAVs as "cowards because they send out
machines to fight us. ... They don\'t want to fight us like real men.
... So we just have to kill a few of their soldiers to defeat them"
(Singer 2009a).

[[Recognition of]{.underline}]{.mark} the [interplay of [masculinities
with technology reveal]{.mark}s [further
developments]{.mark}]{.underline}. As Lauren Wilcox has demonstrated in
her study of offense--defense theory, technologies during World War I
were interpreted, "not on their material contribution to offensive or
defensive combat strategies but instead on their relationship to
[idealized images of soldiers' masculinity bound up in strength,
bravery, and chivalry]{.underline}" (2009, 225). These images of
[warrior masculinity undergo a reconstitution when those prosecuting the
war]{.underline} are tele-present, yet operate at a physical remove,
suggesting new views of how gender works to bring meaning to warfare.
These shifts can be placed in historical perspective, and the unmanning
of warfare, both as an integral part of U.S. military strategy and as
on-going practice in the current military campaign, raises important
questions for hierarchies of masculinity within the armed forces. Steve
Niva argues that the media coverage of the Gulf War of 1990--91 marks a
shift in masculinity, whereby infantrymen "took a backseat in war
coverage to computer programmers, missile technologists, battle-tank
commanders, high-tech pilots, and those appropriately equipped and
educated for new world order warfare" (Niva 1998, 119). For Niva, the
feminization of American manhood caused by the Vietnam War was overcome
by the emergence of a [[new hegemonic military masculinity suited to
technowar conditions]{.underline}]{.mark}, one that "accentuated the
technological and civilizational superiority of the U.S. military and
society" re-asserting the pre-eminence of the Western type of man (1998,
119).

Reconfigurations of masculinity will undoubtedly intersect with
questions of honor and heroism. Feminist scholars have noted the dangers
of abstractions enabled by the use of precision guided munitions,
pointing out how they can foster indifference to the targets and the
illusion of safety, blinding the soldier to the brutality of their
actions (Cohn and Ruddick 2004, 414). The potential for moral
disengagement has long characterized modern warfare, yet the increasing
remove at which war is conducted would seem to have consequences for
some of the values that underwrite the meaning war has for participants.
For instance, the physical safety enjoyed by U.S. drone pilots
complicates honor claims. As one Afghanistan drone pilot comments: "It
sounds strange but being far away and safe is kind of a bummer. The
other guys are exposing themselves, and that to me is still quite an
honorable thing to do. So I feel like I'm cheating them. I'm relatively
safe" (Pitzke 2010). The meaning of heroism under conditions of remote
warfare will likely be subject to renegotiation. In an early indication
of the direction of this transformation, in 2007 Greg Harbin became the
first UAV pilot to receive the Distinguished Flying Cross for piloting
one of the first Predators over Bosnia from his desk in Hungary out of
the path of a school yard of children (Garreau, 2007). Soldiers engaged
in the removal of improvised explosive devices in Iraq also report
forging emotional connections with masculinized, anthromorphized robotic
assistants, giving the robots names, battle commendations, and even
taking them fishing.7 As Tickner, following Elshtain, has noted, a
society\'s war stories are key to galvanizing support for the war
effort; these stories depend on heroism, strength, and other qualities
characterized as masculine (2001, 56--57). What remains to be seen is in
what ways tele-operated combat can continue to be subject to heroic
narrativization.

These configurations of masculinity may need to be negotiated amidst
widening notions of what constitutes "battlespace." Media reports of the
drone program have focused on the ways in which the frontlines of war
have seeped into the domestic space. Representations have highlighted
the domestic lives of Nevada [[UAV pilots]{.underline}]{.mark},
particularly the ways in which these remote operators are [[susceptible
to stress and psychological problems]{.underline}]{.mark} seen in
"normal" combat participants (Zucchino 2010). Adjustment to this
[[encroachment of]{.mark} the [battlespace]{.mark}
[on]{.mark}]{.underline} the [[domestic space]{.mark} may [increase the
burden]{.mark}s [on]{.mark} (typically [female) care-givers]{.mark} on
the homefront, while [adding]{.mark} the [potential]{.mark} for
**[domestic violence]{.mark}.**]{.underline} Further, the circulation of
images of war over networks arguably expands the space of war without
prompting reflection on the ethical effects of mediated proximity. The
circulation of "war porn"---official (and unsanctioned) battlefield
videos taken from the Afghanistan and Iraq conflicts from UAV cameras
often accompanied by heavy metal music and violent commentary---has
become a troubling form of domestic entertainment. Critics argue these
one-sided video representations "with a videogame sensibility ...
fetishize---and warp---the most brutal parts of these high-tech wars"
(Ramirez 2010).

strategy, weapons, and arms control. Since that time, she has also
devoted much of her career to studying nuclear weapons, strategy, and
gender. [Cohn]{.underline} (1987, 687, 696) [found that the men, "and
indeed, they are virtually all men," who work as ["defense
intellectuals"]{.mark} typically [employ]{.mark} language filled with
["sexual imagery."]{.mark}]{.underline} In a lengthy and much-cited
article about her experiences among defense intellectuals, Cohn points
out numerous examples of sexual images and language, [including standard
references to penetration, throw-weight, and spasm attacks]{.underline}
(see also Sylvester 1994, 176). Additionally, Cohn (1987, 694) noted
that "The history of the atomic bomb project itself is rife with overt
images of competitive male sexuality, as is the discourse of the early
nuclear physicists, strategists, and SAC commanders." Based on his
extensive personal experience in the field, nuclear physicist Brian
Easlea (1987, 146) likewise acknowledged that [military scientists and
nuclear strategists employ "frightening" and even "obscene" sexual
[metaphors]{.mark} in an effort [to "demonstrate]{.mark} indisputable
[masculine status."]{.mark}]{.underline}

As demonstrated in the prior section, [Dr. Strangelove is certainly
filled with sexual metaphors and phallic images. Again, "all of the
major characters have names which are either explicitly or subtly
sexually denotative]{.underline}" (Linden 1977, 76). In addition to the
phallic images already noted, General Ripper's scenes often include this
imagery. Notably, Ripper smokes a large phallic cigar in his meeting
with Group Captain Mandrake, which Kubrick made much more prominent by
use of another low-angled camera shot. Later, Ripper fires a long
cylindrical machine gun, another phallic symbol, when his base comes
under attack from American forces trying to learn the codes to recall
the bombers and stop the war he has initiated. In the ending, as Starck
(2010, 61) notes, Major Kong's "positioning on the rocket \[he
straddles\] and the camera angle produce the impression of a giant
penis." Film scholar M. Keith Booker (2007, 103--4) echoes the feminist
critique when he argues that [Kubrick's repeated use of sexual images
and metaphors work to characterize "the Cold War as a phallic
competition" as the military leaders attempt to demonstrate that they
"have the bigger and more effective weapons."]{.underline}

[The second strand of the feminist critique of nuclear weapons focuses
on the **["technostrategic"]{.mark}** (Cohn 1987, 690n)
**[abstractions]{.mark}** employed by specialists working in the field.
The language of defense intellectuals is peppered with euphemisms and
acronyms that [leave out]{.mark} "the emotional, the concrete, the
particular, human bodies and their vulnerability, **[human lives
and]{.mark} their [subjectivity]{.mark}**---all of which are marked
feminine"]{.underline} (Cohn, Hill and Ruddick 2005, 5). [In Dr.
Strangelove,]{.underline} this is most apparent in the language of
General Turgidson, who speaks almost nonchalantly to President Muffley
about the potential death of "ten to twenty million killed, tops." In
this passage, [Turgidson speaks obliquely about the number of American
lives that would be lost after Soviet retaliation to a US first strike.
His additional description of the nuclear attack on the Soviet Union is
just as abstract, not at all focused on the mass murder that would be
committed:]{.underline}

> Mr. President, about thirty-five minutes ago, General Jack Ripper, the
> commanding General of Burpleson Air Force Base, issued an order to the
> 34 B-52's of his wing which were airborne at the time as part of a
> special exercise we were holding called Operation Dropkick. Now, it
> appears that the order called for the planes to attack their targets
> inside Russia. The planes are fully armed with nuclear weapons with an
> average load of 40 megatons each. . . . The aircraft will begin
> penetrating Russian radar cover within 25 minutes.

In response, President Muffley, an effeminate male character, does
describe such an attack as mass murder and reminds Turgidson that the
United States had "avowed . . . never to strike first with nuclear
weapons."8 Turgidson notes technocratically that "General Ripper has
already invalidated that policy." In addition to these exchanges, much
of the military dialogue in the film is filled with mysterious and
abstract acronyms, including the scene on the B-52 when Major Kong and
his crew review the nuclear attack profile.9 They discuss the CRM114,
the ICBM complex at Laputa, and a secondary target at "November Bravo
X-Ray One Zero Eight."

The third strand of the feminist critique is that [[nuclear language
relies upon gendered]{.mark}, specifically masculine, [axioms about
security]{.mark}. Nuclear deterrence is a purposeful
strategy]{.underline}, reflecting the retaliatory destruction states
threaten when facing certain situations. [According to a standard
feminist understanding of IR, this is a ["stereotypically
masculine"]{.mark}]{.underline} (Wright 2010, 206) [perspective. By
viewing [ideas such as rationality, autonomy,]{.mark} and [power as
socially constructed masculine stereotypes]{.mark} rather than as
characteristics of human nature, feminist critics suggest that [IR
concepts]{.mark} that are long taken-for-granted, [**like deterrence,
are** actually **gendered.**]{.mark}]{.underline} Echoing these ideas
from Tickner's (1988) work discussed above, Cohn et al. (2005, 4)
explain how the use of **[[masculine language frames debates about
nuclear weapons]{.mark} and strategy:]{.underline}**

> Ideas about gender also shape, limit and distort the national and
> international political processes through which decisions about WMD
> \[weapons of mass destruction\] are made. [Ideas about strength,
> protection, rationality, security and control have a critical impact
> on governmental and intergovernmental policy, as well as functioning
> at a large-scale societal level, where a certain notion of aggressive
> masculinity is equated with human nature.]{.underline}

These concepts are apparent in the film, as IR scholars like Kuzma and
Haney (2001), Lindley (2001), and Valeriano (2013) emphasize. Strategist
Dr. Strangelove certainly describes and criticizes the Soviet doomsday
device in these terms: "Deterrence is the art of producing in the mind
of the enemy . . . the fear to attack. And so, because of the automated
and irrevocable decision-making process which rules out human meddling,
the doomsday machine is terrifying. It's simple to understand. And
completely credible, and convincing." A few moments later, Strangelove
notes that the "whole point of the doomsday machine is lost if you keep
it a secret!" He asks Ambassador de Sadeski, "Why didn't you tell the
world, eh?" For feminist critics, the practical and pedagogical value
that analysts and scholars have traditionally derived from discussing
and teaching Dr. Strangelove, which emphasize the alleged requirements
of nuclear deterrence, may well reinforce the gender stereotypes
embedded in the concepts.

Despite many decades of important feminist critiques of national
security policies, nuclear armaments, military life, war, and even the
definition of security itself, a large schism continues to divide most
IR academics and the field's feminist scholars (Cohn 2018). Whereas
Sjoberg (2010, 2) finds that [feminist theories have paved the way for
meaningful and even pragmatic changes in the policy world, and gender is
now a "salient concern of global governance," she emphasizes that
**feminists and mainstream security studies scholars rarely communicate.
The result is that national security curriculum, scholarship, and
practice remain reified as masculine spheres**]{.underline} (Zalewski
and Parpart 1998). Charli Carpenter (2007, 341) contends that a good
starting point for addressing this fissure is for scholars to provide
material that allows students learning about the discipline to recognize
the importance of masculinity in IR. Dr. Strangelove is precisely such a
text---and it is already widely used. However, IR and security scholars
would need to acknowledge and discuss sexual and gender-related themes
and images that have long been apparent to film critics and scholars
from other disciplines, even as they incorporate feminist criticisms
from the discipline.

Conclusions

The IR analysts and scholars who continue to discuss the classic film
Dr. Strangelove publicly, and/or utilize it in their classes, typically
reference or use the film to emphasize traditional points about
deterrence and national security. Indeed, as demonstrated, these experts
do not typically grapple with the film's gender themes or "sexual
subtext," though these ideas have long been highlighted by scholars from
other disciplines and film critics. IR experts recognize that Stanley
Kubrick's decision to employ satire allowed him to critique nuclear
deterrence and high-level decision-making, but they typically overlook
Dr. Strangelove's overt comparison of the logic of nuclear deterrence
strategy to absurd male sexual fantasies and exaggerated notions of
masculinity. While [Dr. Strangelove]{.underline} obviously depicts a
male-dominated world, the story does not follow established masculine
tropes of war narratives. It is neither a tragedy nor a heroic romance.
The film is not a straight-forward and serious story about autonomous
states and the men who lead them making rational calculations and
decisions about how to use military power. Those allegedly masculine
traits are referenced in the film, but many of the men are shown to be
irrational, and the autonomy and power of every man and nation is
ultimately challenged by the existence of a doomsday device that is
triggered automatically. [The film satirizes and thereby ridicules
top-level male national security elites by revealing them to be
buffoonish characters whose sexual fantasies and behaviors cannot
readily be separated from their outlandish ideas about nuclear weapons,
deterrence strategy, and war.]{.underline}

The field's failure to emphasize the sexual subtext of Dr. Strangelove
parallels more broadly the lack of scholarly engagement with feminist
contributions to IR and security studies. As both opinion and literature
surveys reveal, IR and security studies scholars do not normally discuss
feminist perspectives in their courses and generally overlook feminist
literature in their research. By acknowledging Dr. Strangelove's
numerous sexual metaphors and images and linking them to feminist
arguments about nuclear weapons and strategy, IR scholars and policy
experts could effectively grapple with the film's wargasm and start to
narrow divisions in the field. Because Dr. Strangelove is such an
important IR text, changing the way it is discussed and taught could
effectively provide a bridge to feminist scholarship about militarized
masculinity, nuclear weapons, and deterrence strategy. Artifacts of
popular culture increasingly influence the field of IR (Daniel and
Musgrave 2017). Scholars should take seriously the wider culture's
understanding of those artifacts--- especially when they so neatly align
with an important and critical subfield in the discipline (Grayson,
Davies, and Philpott 2009, 156).

Finally, as feminists like Cohn have demonstrated, real-world
contemporary depictions of nuclear weapons and strategy reveal an
ongoing need to identify and critique the sexual images and metaphors
still at work in public debates. [If a wider range of scholars
understood and embraced the feminist interpretation of Dr. Strangelove,
**[scholars could]{.mark} publicly [confront]{.mark} such
[depictions]{.mark} and [extend]{.mark} the [feminist critique]{.mark}
well beyond the academic IR classroom.**]{.underline} For example, Cohn
and Ruddick (2004, 422--23) reference an Indian newspaper's political
cartoon published after five 1998 nuclear tests. It featured the caption
"Made with Viagra." They likewise quote a Hindu nationalist leader
saying that the bomb helped "to prove that we are not eunuchs." Beyond
these examples related to nuclear proliferation, defense intellectuals,
military leaders, and prominent politicians continue to talk brazenly
about the links between sexual urges, macho competition, nuclear weapons
systems, and war. Tom Christie, "the Pentagon's chief weapons tester"
during the George W. Bush presidency (2001 to 2005), made sexually
tinged remarks about the latest F-35 stealth aircraft, which is expected
to be fitted with nuclear bombs by 2024: "[There's always this sexual
drive for a new airplane on the part of each \[armed\] service . . .
Persistent, urgent and natural"]{.underline} (quoted in Thompson 2013).
Identifying the same theme, retired General Lee Butler (quoted in Kazel
2015), who served in 1991 as the final commander of the US Strategic Air
Command, recently told an interviewer that nations amass nuclear weapons
for "testosterone-driven" reasons. Similarly, [General Stanley
McChrystal (quoted in Rose 2013, 8) provocatively declared, "If you look
at the role I had in Iraq, it is sexy, it is satisfying, it is manly, it
scratches an itch in the American culture that people
like."]{.underline} Notoriously, of course, the second Iraq war was
initially and primarily justified by the Bush administration because of
fears that Saddam Hussein would acquire nuclear weapons.

Most recently, [President Donald Trump prominently taunted North Korean
leader Kim Jong-un by calling him "little rocket man" and warning that
America would unleash "fire and fury" on North Korea should it make any
new threats against the United States.]{.underline} On January 2, 2018,
Trump tweeted (quoted in Ditum 2018) [that Kim Jong-un "stated that the
'Nuclear Button is on his desk at all times.' Will someone . . . please
inform him that I too have a Nuclear Button, but it is a much bigger &
more powerful one than his, and my Button works!"]{.underline} As Cohn
(2018) opined, [this "nuclear saber-rattling . . . sounded a lot like,
well, penismeasuring."]{.underline} Beatrice Fihn (Miles 2018),
executive director of the policy-oriented International Campaign to
Abolish Nuclear Weapons, criticized Trump for "threatening to mass
murder a whole country" and claimed [that Trump's "politics were imbued
with 'toxic masculinity.']{.underline}" Columnist Sarah Ditum used the
same "toxic masculinity" phrasing to describe Trump's threats but
pointed out that his reference to a nuclear button was "not the most
impressive penis euphemism ever." [These examples reveal that the
lessons about gender and nuclear weapons that can be learned from Dr.
Strangelove remain relevant today, and ongoing [**public debates**
provide ample opportunity for security analysts]{.mark} and IR scholars
[to take the feminist criticisms seriously.]{.mark}]{.underline}

### 2NC\-\--L\-\--Liberalism

#### The 1AC\'s liberal framing is masculinist at its core---its faith in rational self-interest excludes women and erases feminine values like care, nurture, and interdependence from Arctic climate policy.

Auður H **Ingólfsdóttir '11.**\[ Faculty of Social Science, University
of Lapland & University of Iceland\] ""Go North, Young Man"1 ---
Gendered discourses on climate change and security in the Arctic."
Nordia Geographical Publications 40: 4. (pg. 91).
<https://www.academia.edu/30644894/Climate_Change_and_Security_in_the_Arctic_A_Feminist_Analysis_of_Norms_and_Values_Shaping_Climate_Policy_in_Iceland?utm_>
//KKang

[[Liberalism]{.underline}]{.mark}, the main competing theory of
international relations to realism, [[is not free from masculine]{.mark}
[values]{.mark} underpinning basic assumptions]{.underline}, according
to Tickner. While realists emphasize competition and power struggles,
liberals are advocates of free trade and cooperation between states that
will maximize benefits[. According [to liberals, human beings]{.mark}
[are driven by rational self interest]{.mark}]{.underline}. "The
rational economic man" is offered in contrast to "the political man".
But [perhaps [the difference is not]{.mark} so
[great]{.mark}]{.underline}? [[The rational]{.mark} economic
[man]{.mark} [has many similarities to the political ma]{.mark}n, but
**[his aggressive]{.mark} [passions]{.mark}** have been [**tamed** by
the]{.mark} rational **[pursuit of profit]{.mark}**]{.underline}
(Tickner 1992). [[Women are still absent]{.mark} from the picture [and
feminine values]{.mark} related to caring, nurture and service, all of
which are crucial for reproduction and the survival of the younger

#### **The Liberalist paradigm of international relations ignores the interior bounds of the public private sphere, empirically emboldening the masculine 'protector' while simultaneously objectifying the 'weak' feminine and bracketing them out of spaces of competition in the welfare state.**

**Shaver 96** (Shelia Shaver, Honorary professor at UNSW Sydney, Working
Paper No. 68, *Liberalism, Gender and Social Policy*, pg. 3-7, UNSW
Sydney, 1996)

\[https://unsworks.unsw.edu.au/entities/publication/1cffd20b-479a-422e-976b-3b142a7dd734\]//rich

Feminist critiques of liberalism have been addressed primarily to
liberalism in its broad sense. [Feminists see [problems in the dichotomy
between the private and the public]{.mark} which lies at the [**centre**
of liberal thought]{.mark}.]{.underline} [Because it [divides]{.mark}
the private [**domestic** life of women from]{.mark} the [public world
of men]{.mark}, they see this separation as inherently
gendered]{.underline}. [[Liberalism]{.mark} treats the [public and
private spheres as independent of one another]{.mark} when in reality
[they are inextricably connected]{.mark}, and so [obscures]{.mark} a
fundamental [source of inequality and oppression]{.mark} in relations
[between the sexes]{.mark}]{.underline}. Pateman (1989a) argues that the
liberal separation is actually two-fold, with the division between
public and private being concealed 4 within the larger one of state and
civil society. F[eminist critiques of the public/private split have
taken varying form, as a dichotomy between nature and culture, between
morality and power, and between the personal and the
political]{.underline}. Feminism's response to liberalism is complicated
by the fact that its own origins lie in the liberal tradition. Its claim
that woman is an independent being is premised on the eighteenthcentury
liberal conception of the independent and autonomous self. While
mainstream feminism arose as a recognisably liberal movement, its
socialist, radical, cultural variants have shared many liberal tenets
(Eisenstein, 1981: 4). All feminisms challenge the liberal separation of
public and private spheres of social life to some degree.

The individualism and universalism of liberal thought are both flawed on
this account. [[Liberal **individualism**]{.mark} acknowledges [only the
bounded self of the public sphere]{.mark}, and so [denies
recognition]{.mark} to the interdependencies and [social
**connectedness** of human society]{.mark} that are relatively more
[important]{.mark} in the [lives of women]{.mark} than of men (Graham,
1983; Hartsock, 1983: 252-9]{.underline}). Gilligan (1982) refers to
this as an 'ethic of care' in women's lives. [[Liberalism's claim to be
universalistic is false]{.mark} on the same account, for the [liberal
individual]{.mark} tacitly [refers only to the **male actor** of the
public sphere]{.mark}]{.underline}. J[ones (1990) takes this line of
argument further. She suggests that the [liberal citizen is modelled
on]{.mark} the behaviour of **[white male elites]{.mark}**, measuring
all citizens against a standard **defined** by particular race, gender
and class characteristics.]{.underline} She specifies three dimensions
in which the particulars of male identity are falsely universalised: the
body in both its symbolic and corporeal senses; the division of social
life into public and private domains in which the sphere of private life
is perceived as 'outside' both state and civil society; [and the
**recognition** of political behaviour only in the competitive,
individual terms of electoral activity, [**obscuring** women's relations
to the state as claimants and low-level service
workers]{.mark}]{.underline}.

[These [problems]{.mark} also [weaken liberalism's]{.mark} claims to be
[egalitarian and meliorist]{.mark}. How can it [treat individuals as
having equal worth without recognising the particularities of individual
and group identity]{.mark}]{.underline} (Vogel, 1988: 136; Yeatman,
1994)? How can social and political institutions be improved without
such recognition? As Jones puts it:

5 even if women achieve juridical/legal equality, gain more adequate
political representation in law-making and administrative bodies, and
possess the economic means and personal motivation to practice their
rights; [and even if women's duties to the state are **broadened** to
include military service, women's **membership** in the political
community still will be **less full** than men's.]{.underline} (Jones,
1990: 782)

Feminist critiques of liberalism have taken sharpest form in the
discussion of the welfare state. In 'The Patriarchal Welfare State',
Pateman maintains that, 'since the early twentieth century, welfare
policies have reached across from public to private and helped uphold a
patriarchal structure of familial life' (1989b: 183; see also Wilson,
1979). [Pateman argues that [welfare states have]{.mark} formed around
[ideals of citizenship based on **'independence'**]{.mark}, in which
independence is [defined by masculine attributes]{.mark} and abilities.
[Women's citizenship]{.mark} is [framed as womanly
**dependence**]{.mark} and [is]{.mark} accordingly
**[defective]{.mark}**.]{.underline} It is also riddled with paradoxes
and contradictions. [Pateman identifies [three elements of
independence]{.mark} through [which patriarchal structures]{.mark} are
**encoded** [in welfare state citizenship]{.mark}: the capacity to [bear
arms]{.mark}, the capacity to [own **property**]{.mark} including
[property in one's own labour]{.mark}, [and]{.mark} the capacity for
[self-government]{.mark}, including [as protector]{.mark} of the family
unit]{.underline}. Pateman sees women's claims to full citizenship as
fraught with 'Wollstonecraft's dilemma', in [which demands for
gender-neutral inclusion on equal terms with men seem to **conflict**
with wishes for recognition of gender-specific talents, needs and
concerns]{.underline}. Pateman sees the welfare state as oppressing
women, but at the same time also responsible for important improvements
in women's circumstances and democratic opportunities. Whatever its
flaws, the welfare state has given women a degree of choice in their
economic dependence on men, and opened the matter of their rights to
public politics.

Fraser (1987) similarly finds a [[gendered]{.mark} **[division]{.mark}**
between public and private at the heart of welfare state provision,
arguing that this division [underlies the distinction between social
insurance and social assistance programs]{.mark} typical [of]{.mark}
most [welfare states]{.mark}]{.underline}. Fraser suggests that [social
insurance [programs construct beneficiaries]{.mark} as rights [bearing
**possessive** individuals]{.mark} and hence [paradigmatically
**male**]{.mark}, while [social **assistance**]{.mark} arrangements
[frame]{.mark} theirs as [clients]{.mark} and [paradigmatically
**female**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Fraser 6 sees this as a double
distinction, in that the rights of social insurance recipients have a
contractual status while the entitlements of social assistance claimants
subject them to the guiding authority of welfare officers. In pointing
to the mediating role of administrative authority, she is less
optimistic about the democratic potential of the welfare state (Yeatman,
1990: 139-48). Fraser's argument leans heavily on the American example,
and the nature of both social insurance and social assistance varies a
good deal from one country to another (Eardley, 1996). It refers to one
of potentially various ways in which gender may be built into welfare
state structuring of public and private life.

Writing from the background of more highly developed welfare states,
Scandinavian feminists have seen the critiques of English-speaking
feminists as too negative about the role of social policy institutions
in the lives of women. Like Pateman, they have seen women's reliance on
the state as an improvement on circumstances in which they are dependent
on individual men (Hernes, 1988: 188; Siim, 1988: 182). In the same way,
they have seen women's active political participation as a potential
counter to the rendering of women as clients of welfare state authority.
Piven (1990) makes the same case with respect to the United States.

Hernes (1988: 202-09) in particular argues that Scandinavian social
policy arrangements bridge the liberal division between public and
private life. In these countries the mobilisation and political
incorporation of women in the second phase of welfare state development
has led to a 'public/private mix'. While these writers are not
uncritical of social policy in the countries of the region (Waerness,
1984; Leira, 1992), the comparative accounts suggest that Scandinavian
welfare states differ from others in this respect. In much of Catholic
Europe the tradition of subsidiarity secured the autonomy of the family
against intrusion by the state, often assigning a continuing welfare
role to church and voluntary bodies The German and Dutch welfare states
follow this pattern though the French is more ambiguous (Flora, 1986:
xviii; Esping-Andersen, 1990: 27; Borchorst, 1994: 32-5; Gustafsson,
1994: 53-6; Ostner, 1993: 103-6; Hantrais, 1993). Siim (1988: 176-9)
distinguishes between women's dependency on the welfare state as clients
and as consumers, and suggests that their dependency as clients
predominates in Britain and the USA while dependency as consumers is
more the norm in Denmark and Sweden. She regards the Danish welfare 7
state as having fostered a partnership between state and family, and to
some extent between women and the state, while the British welfare state
still largely leaves the support of reproductive life to the family.

### 2NC\-\--L\-\--Realism

#### The 1AC maps hegemonic masculinity onto state behavior, reflecting a patriarchal tradition in IR that privileges the glorified male warrior.
