[intersectionality]{.underline} has been [theoretical]{.underline},
increasingly scholars (and activists) have been turning their attention
towards putting the various commitments of intersectionality into
practice. This section focuses on [intersectionality [as
**methodology**]{.mark}]{.underline}, both [[for **research** and
**activism**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

While there is not one way of [conducting intersectional
research]{.underline}, there are a few places where it might be
[incorporated within feminist methodologies]{.underline}. A distinctive
feature of [[feminist and critical methodologies]{.underline}]{.mark} is
the [[emphasis on **reflexivity**]{.underline}]{.mark} -- the need for
[[researchers]{.underline}]{.mark} to continually
[[reflect]{.underline}]{.mark} on their [[**positionality** and
power]{.mark} throughout the research process]{.underline} (Ackerly,
Stern, and True 2006). Often, research [reflexivity focuses
on]{.underline} the [power researchers]{.underline} hold in the research
process and the ways in which they may (intentionally or
unintentionally) [[oppress research subjects and cause
harm]{.underline}]{.mark}. This is important, and [[scholars need to be
**conscientious** of]{.underline}]{.mark} this, as well as the other
**[[power dynamics]{.underline}]{.mark}** that may be [[present]{.mark}
in research relationships]{.underline}. There is a much work in
[feminist methodology]{.underline} that [focuses]{.underline}
[on]{.underline} the ways of [[mediating power throughout the research

**group participation**]{.underline}]{.mark} in the
[research]{.underline} in ways that [[allow for **representation and
agency**]{.underline}]{.mark} (both of which are important [components
of intersectional praxis]{.underline}).

[[Intersectional reflexivity]{.underline}]{.mark} might also
[[reflect]{.underline}]{.mark} on the [particular [positionality of the
researcher]{.mark}]{.underline} and the impact it may have on the
research. Reflexivity in this way is an [important part of being a good
critical thinker]{.underline}. Our [positionality]{.underline} may
actually [prevent]{.underline} us from seeing relevant [[power
dimensions]{.mark} if we [are not]{.mark} [**intentional** about
uncovering them]{.mark}]{.underline}. [[Intersectional work]{.mark}
coming [from]{.mark} the **[feminist standpoint]{.mark}**
tradition]{.underline} (a [[feminist theoretical
perspective]{.underline}]{.mark} that argues knowledge is socially
situated) argued the [[recognition of]{.mark} **[epistemological
privilege]{.mark}**]{.underline} -- that [those experiencing
oppression]{.underline} have a better understanding of the structures
and institutions that created those conditions. Humans are more aware of
environmental factors that work against them than those that work with
and for them. We are more likely to notice a slope when we are moving up
it than down it; when the wind is in our face than when it is at our
back. Thus, as researchers (and as activists and human beings), it is
useful to be reflexive on our own positionality in relations to others
in thinking through tensions that arise from conflicting understandings
and narratives.

In our analysis, we might want to consider a methodology that Matsuda
(1991, 1189), a legal theorist, simply calls 'ask the other question'.
She goes on to explain, 'when I see something that looks racist, I ask,
"Where is the patriarchy in this?" When I see something that look
sexist, I ask, "Where is the heterosexism in this?" When I see something
that looks homophobic, I ask, "Where are the class interests in this?"'
Her work focuses on [[building **coalitions** based
on]{.underline}]{.mark} what is essentially an [[intersectional
analysis]{.underline}]{.mark}, looking for both obvious and non-obvious
relationships of domination. Here, the work done by the scholars and
activists above can inform what questions we ask. What are the
[[dimensions of power]{.mark} that have been
[theorised]{.mark}]{.underline} or discussed by differently positioned
groups? How can we attend to them in our research? How might we be on
the lookout for additional dimensions relevant to the context? How might
we be complicit in [[enacting oppression]{.underline}]{.mark}, even if
we are unaware of doing so? And given that we might not be able to
identify our complicity, even when we are trying to [be
[reflexive]{.mark}]{.underline}, how can we respond to
[[critiques]{.underline}]{.mark} that are [[levied]{.mark} against us
[by]{.mark}]{.underline} those who may have the [[epistemological
privilege]{.underline}]{.mark}?

Beyond these general guidelines, which we should all be thinking about,
there are some **[[limitations]{.underline}]{.mark}** to what we can do
on a [particular [research project]{.mark}]{.underline}. And, as
mentioned earlier in the chapter, [[single-axis]{.mark}
[interventions]{.mark} are [not]{.mark} inherently
[bad]{.mark}]{.underline}; they are [[important]{.underline}]{.mark} and
sometimes **[[necessary]{.underline}]{.mark}**, particularly in
[state-centred [understandings]{.mark} of power relations]{.underline}
which often '[[render invisible]{.underline}]{.mark} or inconsequential
the racialized, gendered, and [[**class nature of power** in
IR]{.underline}]{.mark}' (Chowdhry and Nair 2003).
[Intersectional]{.underline} scholars (in the various traditions --
including postcolonial) have discussed the importance of
'**[[strategic]{.mark} [essentialism]{.mark}]{.underline}**[']{.mark},
which is the [[idea]{.underline}]{.mark} that a particular [[dimension
of oppression]{.underline}]{.mark} (or even a particular inter- section)
may be deployed contingently and strategically. This may be [[important
for]{.mark} scholarly]{.underline} [[**interventions** as well as in
organising **collective action**]{.underline}]{.mark}. The point is that
it is deliberate and in [[recognition of its limitations as well as the

The [[concept of powe]{.mark}r]{.underline} is central to the [[study of

#### Engaging in [intersectionality]{.underline} solves by [destabilizing]{.underline} slow and epistemic violence

**Väyryenen '21** \[Tarja, Swati Parasher, Élise Féron, Catia Cecilia;

**[is more accurate]{.mark}** and leads the authors to develop deeper

### Alt\-\--Ethic of Care

#### Embrace a feminist ethic of care that accepts the embodied other and understands the complex context in which foreign policy is crafted as oppose the rigid rational patriarchal modes of IR reasoning that frame the affs foreign policy. 

**Robinson** (Fiona Robinson, Professor of Political Science
specializing in International Relations and Political Theory, MA in
International Affairs, BA Honours in Political Studies PHD from
Cambridge.). Feminist foreign policy as ethical foreign policy? A care
ethics perspective. Journal of International Political Theory, 17(1),
20-37. 2021-2-1 https://doi.org/10.1177/1755088219828768//Bend

Feminist care ethics and feminist foreign policy

In this final section, I want to suggest how an [[ethic of care]{.mark}
might [inform]{.mark} a [feminist foreign policy]{.mark} in ways that
allow it [to challenge]{.mark}, rather than reinforce, [gender]{.mark}ed
[binaries]{.mark} [between realism and idealism, order and justice and
masculine and feminine]{.mark}.]{.underline} I suggest that there are
three attributes of an [ethic of care]{.mark} that [allow]{.mark} it to
achieve this: [[relationality, contextualism]{.mark} and
[revisability]{.mark}]{.underline}.

As seen by Carol Gilligan, one of the first theorists of
[['care']{.mark} [as]{.mark} a voice of [**resistance to dominant**
modes]{.mark} of ethical reasoning, the ethics of care sees people not
as 'standing alone', but as gaining their selfhood through their
relations with others]{.underline}. Self and other are different but
connected rather than separate and opposed (Gilligan, 1993: 147).
Morality, on this view, is about responding to the needs of others in
ways that are characterized by attentive listening, patience and
understanding. Universal moral principles of right -- so [[rational,
clean]{.mark} and [appealing]{.mark} -- [give way]{.mark} here
[to]{.mark} the [messy, relentless]{.mark} juggling act of navigating
[complex moral dilemmas]{.mark} [and balanci]{.mark}ng the
[competing]{.mark} needs of real, **[embodied
others]{.mark}**.]{.underline} This means constant re-evaluation of
beliefs and reflexivity regarding our own claims to knowledge. It is a
feminist ethics, not because it 'belongs' to women or because it is
anti-men or somehow against the ethics of justice, but because it
demands a [[question]{.mark}ing of the [script of
patriarchy]{.mark}.]{.underline} As Gilligan puts it, in the culture of
patriarchy, the different voice with its ethic of care sounds feminine.
Heard in its own right and on its own terms, it is a human voice
([Gilligan,
2011](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/1755088219828768#bibr6-1755088219828768):
25).

Relationality has been a key feature of the ethics of care since Carol
Gilligan's first edition of In a Different Voice. On this view, all
selves are the product of relations, all the way down. [The apparently
autonomous self, so often seen in men and gendered masculine, is a
product not of some essential feature of 'manhood' but of a response by
men to conform to the codes of gender within patriarchy.]{.underline}
Over the past 40 years, research into human psychology has undergone a
radical shift towards accepting the interpersonal and relational nature
of human development ([Gilligan and Snider,
2017](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/1755088219828768#bibr7-1755088219828768):
191). Recognizing this and [[accepting]{.mark} the [relation]{.mark}al
voice]{.underline} not as morally or psychologically immature, but
[[as]{.mark} a [human voice]{.mark} that is [thwarted by]{.mark}
pervasive [gender norms]{.mark}]{.underline}, is the first step towards
[[reach]{.mark}ing [across divides]{.mark}]{.underline} of difference
hierarchy and [[build]{.mark}ing [real connection]{.mark} with
others]{.underline}.

[[Relationality is not]{.mark} a [static]{.mark} ethical
concept]{.underline} or set of principles -- like 'rights' or 'justice'
-- rather, it is a [[way of seeing]{.mark} the world
[that]{.mark}]{.underline} [[addresses]{.mark} not only
[ontology]{.mark}]{.underline} -- the relational self -- [[but also
epistemology]{.underline}]{.mark}. Thus, knowledge is also understood
relationally -- [we must [ask who makes]{.mark} the [knowledge
claims]{.mark} and [from what]{.mark} vantage point]{.underline}, what
[[material circumstances]{.underline}]{.mark} [and]{.mark} what [[degree
of power]{.underline}]{.mark} (Minow, 1990: 178). As famously argued by
Martha Minow, [relationality is perhaps most significant]{.underline} in
the way that it shifts our [understanding of the problem of
'difference']{.underline}; seeing [difference relationally means a shift
from a focus on the distinctions between people to a focus on the
relationships within]{.underline} which we notice and draw distinctions
([Minow,
1990](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/1755088219828768#bibr23-1755088219828768):
15). Thus, [[relational]{.mark} [approach]{.mark}es, [unlike]{.mark},
say, [dominant liberal]{.mark} rights [analysis]{.mark}, enquire into
the institutional practices that determine a norm against which some
people seem different, or deviant]{.underline}. To address
relationships, Minow (1990) argues, is to [[resist abstraction
and]{.mark} to [demand context]{.mark}]{.underline} (1990: 216).

Clearly, there is a close connection between relationality and context.
[An [ethic of care eschews universalizable]{.mark} moral
[principles]{.mark}]{.underline} that can be applied across time and
space. By contrast, it [demands attention to context]{.underline} -- to
the particularities of social location, historical background,
structural conditions and relationships between relevant moral actors.
In this sense, it is opposed to the logic of traditional moral theory,
which demands abstraction from context in order to gain objectivity. But
this [[objectivity]{.mark} is elusive]{.underline}; more than this, [it
[acts to create]{.mark} a [dichotomy between those who]{.mark} are the
[know]{.mark}ers, keepers [and enforce]{.mark}rs]{.underline} of moral
principles, [[and those who are compelled]{.mark} -- sometimes [through
'foreign policy']{.mark}]{.underline} -- to enact those principles. The
morality of 'arms deals' cannot be assessed outside of the context of
both the violent histories of colonialism and the liberal militarism
that defines the contemporary world. Feminist foreign policy will not be
transformative if it is reduced to enacting a set of moral principles,
or seeking to protect, promote or empower women (often because this is,
in the long run, good for 'national security' or 'economic growth'). A
foreign policy that works towards feminist goals is more likely to be a
slow, plodding process which considers historical and contemporary
relations between actors and recognizes the importance of context in
making decisions and policies.

Finally, [[revisability]{.mark} [refers to]{.mark} the [requirement of
epistemological humility]{.mark}]{.underline} -- the need to embrace
uncertainty and recognize that there are only better or worse courses of
action at any given time and in any context. When 'Amy', one of
Gilligan's (1993) subjects, responds to a question posed regarding an
abstract moral dilemma, she says, 'Well, it depends' (1993: 35). As
Gilligan explains, when considered in the light of Kohlberg's definition
of the stages and sequence of moral development, her moral judgements
appear to be a full stage lower in maturity than those of the boy. They
appear to reveal an inability to think systematically about the concepts
of morality or law and a reluctance to examine the logic of received
moral truths (Gilligan, 1993: 30). But from the perspective of an ethic
of care, Amy's reluctance to make universalized judgements stems from a
heightened perception of the role of context and the nature of
relationships in moral judgement.

It is this willingness to [live with [uncertainty]{.mark}]{.underline}
that [[defines **feminist care ethics** as critical]{.underline}]{.mark}
and will allow it to be, potentially, [transformative [in the long
term]{.mark}]{.underline}. As Kimberly Hutchings (2001) argues,
'Critique is premised on the impossibility of a definitive answer to the
conditions of its own possibility and can only content itself with the
acknowledgment of the revisability of any grounds on which its specific
claims are based' (2001: 90). The implication of this is that we must
[[let go of]{.mark} the idea of feminist foreign policy as '[principled
foreign policy']{.mark}]{.underline}. Principles have an unmistakable
allure; they work very well when we take what Raymond Geuss (2008) calls
an 'ethics-first' approach to politics -- where we [attain an [ideal
theory]{.mark} [of]{.mark} how we should act]{.underline}, and then, in
a second step, [apply that ideal theory to the [action of political
agents]{.mark}]{.underline} (2008: 8). But the [dominance of this kind
of thinking [in]{.mark} the [realm of]{.mark} 'ethical [foreign
policy']{.mark} has [led to]{.mark} a
'[**hyper-rationalist**']{.mark}]{.underline} (Rengger, 2000: 769)
[[approach to]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[suffering]{.mark} of the
peoples of the world, [where '**useful knowledge'**]{.mark} has [blinded
us to]{.mark} contingency, [context, embodiment and
emotion]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Revisability in ethics is not the same thing as moral relativism. As
Susan Hekman points out, the category of 'relativism' is parasitic on
its opposite, the possibility of absolute knowledge. Perspectival,
connected, discursive knowledge does not obviate the possibility of
truth, evidence or critical judgement (Hekman, 1995: 31). It does not
mean that states cannot make judgements in their foreign (and domestic)
policy about the allocation of resources towards programmes and policies
that aim to increase the participation and representation of women in,
for example, formal peacemaking and peace-building processes. There is a
[requirement, however, to [view each policy]{.mark} and programme on its
own terms and [in its own context]{.mark}]{.underline} and to
[[recognize]{.underline}]{.mark} the ever-changing [context of
[actors-in-relation]{.mark} [across]{.mark}]{.underline} multiple,
intersecting locations and scales -- across [[racial,]{.mark}
socio-economic [and ethnic divides]{.mark}]{.underline} and from the
household [[to the **'global' level**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

### Alt\-\--Queer Art of Failure

#### Vote affirmative to endorse the queer art of failure in IR.s

In this way, [not only do [traditional]{.mark} standards of
[knowledge]{.mark} make invisible their own impossibility, they
[hide]{.mark} **the [violence of IR's denials of failure]{.mark}** and
continued insistence on traveling failed paths despite the condemnation
of failure and the privileging of success]{.underline}. [IR's
**continued recursive [enactment of]{.mark} its settled
["standards"]{.mark}** []{.mark}despite their **obvious failure and

[sexed impacts]{.mark}** []{.mark}of those **standards [and]{.mark}
their apparent objectivity**]{.underline}. Baudrillard's work provides a
path for navigating this disjuncture between signs (IR's "standards")
and referents (the fantastic notion that "good scholarship" exists
objectively) (Baudrillard, 1975). He argues that "[only **ambivalence,
as a rupture of value**... sustains a **challenge to the legibility,**
the false transparency of the sign... questions the evidence of the use
value of the sign (rational decoding) and of its exchange value (the
discourse of communication)]{.underline}." [This]{.underline}
ambivalence, Baudrillard argues, "[brings the political economy of the
sign to a standstill; it dissolves the respective definitions of symbol
and referent]{.underline}" (Baudrillard, 1981, p. 150). [Endorsing the
inclusion of nontraditional perspectives, classifying and categorizing
them, and **assuming inclusion's possibility** may all have violent
impacts]{.underline} (for discussions of the violences of inclusion see
Haritaworn et al., 2013, 2014; Mbembe, 2019). Moving of the
signification "knowledge" from any referent to which it was originally
tied makes method and research performances of scholarship, rather than
(the illusive) scholarship itself. If "research" is a performance of
scholarship, ["**[standards" for research]{.mark}** serve to
**[disguise]{.mark} the [fantastic nature of knowledge
cumulation]{.mark}**]{.underline}. As such, [there is no space for
liminality[,]{.mark} uncertainty, change, inadequacy, and failure in
structural rather than passing senses]{.underline}. Yet looking beyond
the discourse of certainty, those pervade IR. A Baudrillardian
[ambivalence toward research programs and their truth statements can
reveal the recursivity of IR's standards of knowledge
cumulation]{.underline}. This is because [condemnation or rejection of
any given research program and its truth statements endorses its
assumptions about truth, as well as some of its assumptions about what
the international arena is and how it works.]{.underline} As such, the
idea that IR knowledge cumulation can be nothing but fantasy is
straightforward. [If the **[reification of standards o]{.mark}f
knowledge cumulation** [is a **signification divorced from a
referent**]{.mark}, where the recovery of the referent is conceptually
and practically impossible, then knowledge cumulation is and will
**always remain an empty signifier**.]{.underline} The only question is
how that empty signifier directs and is directed. We suggest that, in
**[IR]{.underline}**, more often than not, [knowledge cumulation
**directs and is directed by discursive seduction**]{.underline}. In
Baudrillard's words, [seduction is]{.underline} "[that which **extracts
meaning from discourse** and **detracts it from its
truth**]{.underline}" (Baudrillard, 1991\[1979\], p. 54). [What makes a
discourse of knowledge, of science, and of progress seductive "is its
very appearance: the aleatory, meaningless, or ritualistic and
meticulous circulation of signs on the surface, its inflections and its
nuances. All of this effaces the content value of meaning, and this is
seductive]{.underline}" (Baudrillard, 1991\[1979\], p. 54). Therefore,
if there could be an interpretive discourse of knowledge cumulation that
reached truth value, that truth value would be self-defeating, since
"[the meaning of an interpretive discourse, by contrast, has never
seduced anyone]{.underline}." This is the fundamental contradiction, in
Baudrillard's terms, that makes standards for knowledge cumulation in IR
internally impossible. He explains that "[every interpretive discourse
wants to get beyond appearances; this is its illusion and fraud. But
getting beyond appearances is appearance, and is hence subject to the
stakes imposed by seduction, and consequently to its own failure as
discourse]{.underline}" (Baudrillard, 1991\[1979\], p. 54). As such,
[what is left in/of the failed discourse can only be the fantastic, and
pretensions to success hollow.]{.underline} Inhabiting a Failure of a
Discipline [The need for success and the denial of failure depend

normative relationship between success and failure.]{.underline} In IR,
as we discussed above, scholars are thought of as successes or failures
according to a complicated metric of disciplinary prestige based on
publication outlet, "scientific standards," and perception of the change
that they have caused in the discipline. [Work is characterized as
successful or failed based on whether it contributes to knowledge
cumulation. The discipline itself is understood as successful or failed
based on the aggregation of those measures.]{.underline} [On these

us, the identification of success/failure is impossible. Still, [we

in different words---a willingness to drop commitment to and passion for
a certain end in the recognition that both that end and its opposite are

[Realizing the caging nature of the boundaries of disciplinary success,

state-of-the-field debates are alternatively narrativized fairy tales,

with a singular end** or an exercise in progressive knowledge

Halberstam, making the queer theory case for celebrating failure, has
become a very successful academic by many of the traditional
disciplinary standards, and The Queer Art of Failure is a successful
academic text. Many of the critics and supporters cited here have built
on academic success by their engagement with the argument. We are
bringing the case to IR, publishing it in a academic journal, through
the traditional mechanism of peer review, from the comfort of secure and
permanent academic employment. We poke holes where those holes can be
seen by those who would police the boundaries between winners and
losers. This article does not "practice" as it "preaches," to itself
upend the "winners" and "losers" in disciplinary IR---it is couched in
both necessary and unnecessary hypocrisy. [But positionally at the very
least, our celebration of failure is itself a failure---implicated in
our interest in and disciplinary IR's promotion of success. We are not
arguing that failure can be made pure or that it should be---only that
it cannot and should not be escaped, especially for the glorification of
success as its perceived opposite]{.underline}. [As we fail at failure
to demonstrate the fantasy of success, we envision a happily ever after
of failing, un-discipline, and diversity rather than a faux idealism of
synthesis, aggrandizement, "science," and disciplining.]{.underline}

### Alt\-\--Ecofem

#### Adopting a feminist perspective enables [sustainable policymaking]{.underline} that solves warming comparatively [better]{.underline}.

Audur H **Ingólfsdóttir**, 20**11**, \"'Go North, Young Man' ---
Gendered discourses on climate change and security in the Arctic\",
Nordia Geographical Publications 40: 4, 89--98, Head of the Climate
Change and Circular Economy Department at the Icelandic Environment and
Energy Agency.

https://nordia.journal.fi/article/view/75952/37327 // asw

As has been discussed in this paper, [**[values and beliefs]{.mark}**
are important in how we view the world, and can be an
**[influential]{.mark}** factor [in how **policy** is **shaped and
implemented**]{.mark}]{.underline}. In this light, [[exploring the
values **underlying discourses** on climate change and security]{.mark}
in the Arctic is an **[important step]{.mark}** in our efforts **[to
find]{.mark}** innovative [**solutions** to]{.mark} new [security
challenges associated with c]{.mark}limate [c]{.mark}hange]{.underline}.
An argument has been presented, that [dominant theories in international
relations are based on a partial view of human nature that is
stereotypically masculine]{.underline}. There is a [[need for a world
view]{.mark} that is more [inclusive of the feminine]{.mark}
characteristics, emphasizing both the conflictive and cooperative
elements of human nature]{.underline}. The focus of attention is
important because it not only [influences how policy is shaped but it
[directs]{.mark} what [types of policy]{.mark} measures are implemented.
By [employing]{.mark} a [feminist perspective, policy]{.mark}
interventions can be more targeted, and [**more likely** to
address]{.mark} the [real security needs]{.mark} of people that are at
risk. This perspective gives hope for an [international
community]{.mark} that is [more **cooperative**]{.mark}, capable of
prioritizing long term **common** benefits over short term
**individual** gains.]{.underline}

#### Vote Neg for Intersectional Feminist Ecology - It's the only way to correctly repay the earth for the sins of patriarchally induced climate disaster.

The foundations for food justice have been growing for decades in the

Overlapping with food justice, the Transition Town movement, named in
1998 and formally launched by 2005, has spread from its origins in the

[This discussion will demonstrate, in the ways outlined above, the depth
and range of feminist perspectives on power]{.underline}---a prime

thus as the beginning and end of investigation and knowledge-building.
[[Feminism requires]{.mark} an [ontological]{.mark} revisionism: a
[**recognition** that]{.mark} it [is necessary to]{.mark} go behind the

[reality]{.mark}.]{.underline} While it may be empirically accurate to

[inequalities]{.mark} that shape them. Mainstream International

[in]{.mark} the worlds of [diplomacy and]{.mark} military and foreign
[policy-making]{.mark}?']{.underline} Tickner, like Enloe, has

### Alt\-\--Ethic of Care

#### Ethic of care solves\-\--it's the only way to interrogate implications and transform policy

**Hankivksy '13** \[Elina; 03/11/2013; University Lecturer in
International Relations at the University of Tampere, Finland; Joy and
International Relations: A New Methodology, P. 68-69; akshaj\]

In our private lives, the [[need for care]{.mark} is generally [expected
and understood]{.mark}]{.underline}. [Political [theory and social
policy]{.mark}]{.underline}, however, have traditionally [located
[care]{.mark}]{.underline} both "[beyond]{.underline} (or
[[beneath]{.underline}]{.mark}) [[politics and]{.underline}]{.mark} the
[[public]{.underline}]{.mark} sphere."1 Public
[[responsibility]{.underline}]{.mark} for care -- [care in the domain of
**[impersonal relations]{.mark}**]{.underline} -- is not widely
recognized or accepted. In contrast, many [feminist
theorists]{.underline} have attempted to [[demonstrate]{.mark} that
[care]{.mark}]{.underline} is [publicly and politically
relevant]{.underline}. As a result of their work, the value of care is
being increasingly recognized. In the context of [political
theory]{.underline}, an **[[ethic of care]{.underline}]{.mark}** has
brought to the [public for [dimensions]{.mark}]{.underline} of our lives
that have been largely uninvestigated. An ethic of care privileges
networks of human [[interdependencies that]{.mark} challenge the
public/private divide]{.underline} and the [[concomitant
role]{.underline}]{.mark} that care plays [[within such
relations]{.underline}]{.mark}. It emphasizes that across our lifespan
-- at all stages and in many situations -- we need care to "sustain the
best possible lives."2 From this [[foundation]{.mark} emerges a set of
[distinct values]{.mark}]{.underline} for [guiding]{.underline} our
[social lives]{.underline} and understanding the [[entire
spectrum]{.mark} of human experiences]{.underline} and human needs. The
values can be considered [[essential]{.underline}]{.mark} to living a
worthwhile, fulling, and [balanced life]{.underline}.

Although the theory continues to develop, [less attention]{.underline}
has been paid [to [care's **practical
implications**]{.mark}]{.underline}. While a small number of feminist
theorists have begun to seriously [[interrogate the
implications]{.mark}]{.underline} of the care [ethic's distinct values
for the public sphere]{.underline},3 to date there have been few
systematic [investigations]{.underline} of how social policy could be
[[transformed by a care ethic]{.underline}]{.mark}. As Iris Marion Young
has noted, "the ethics of care has done little in terms of applying its
[insights]{.underline} to the [[pressing social
policy]{.underline}]{.mark} issues of [[justice]{.underline}]{.mark} and
needs that face all [societies]{.underline} in the world."4 The
explorations of this book are meant to address the gap between
[theoretical [and **public policy analysis**]{.mark}]{.underline} by
considering the [[implications]{.underline}]{.mark} of [an [ethic of
care]{.mark}]{.underline} for a range of Canadian social policy issues.

The [[fundamental question]{.underline}]{.mark} that
[underpins]{.underline} the argument of [[Social Policy and the Ethic of
Care]{.mark}]{.underline} is "What are the consequences of the human
need for care in social policy?" In answering this question I consider
the values that we currently prioritize in the public sphere to
understand why we are making and justifying speciWc social policy
choices. If we start with the [[assumption]{.underline}]{.mark} that the
[need for [care]{.mark}]{.underline} should be taken seriously, then the
[status quo]{.underline}, [[developed]{.underline}]{.mark} in accordance
wit[h [the values and priorities of a **liberal justice
orientation**]{.mark}]{.underline}, is not acceptable. The limitations
of our current vision become illuminated and the need to
[rethink]{.underline} our values in [[relation to society, citizenship,
and future policy]{.underline}]{.mark} directions becomes clear.
Arguably, the inclusion of a care ethic can lead to [greater social
justice in social policy]{.underline} because it opens up new ways of
seeing human beings, their social problems, and their needs, and it
enables us to analyze critically how [government responds]{.underline}
to these. Specially, I intend to demonstrate how, from the [[perspective
of the care ethic]{.underline}]{.mark}, the centrality of [human
interdependence and care is [revealed]{.mark}]{.underline}.

From this [basis]{.underline}, I attempt to show that an [[ethic of care
prioritizes]{.underline}]{.mark}, with its very [distinct **[normative
framework]{.mark}**]{.underline}[,]{.mark} contextualizing the [human
condition]{.underline}, thereby giving [new meaning and
significance]{.underline} to [human differences]{.underline} that [arise
[from]{.mark}]{.underline} [[gender]{.underline}]{.mark}, class, race,
ethnicity, [sexuality]{.underline}, ability, and geographic location.
Because of its [[sensitivity and responsiveness]{.underline}]{.mark} to
other persons' **[[individual differences]{.underline}]{.mark}**,
uniqueness, and whole particularity,5 a care ethic opens new ways for
[[understanding]{.mark} experiences]{.underline} of [discrimination,
suffering, and oppression]{.underline}. And, Wnally, because it
[[rejects indifference to social]{.underline}]{.mark} [[consequences of
actions and decisions]{.underline}]{.mark}, an [ethic of
care]{.underline} can provide [valuable guidance]{.underline} for
governments and [social institutions]{.underline} to create more
[humane, effective, and [robust]{.mark} social
[policies]{.mark}]{.underline}. In sum, a care ethic
[enhances]{.underline} our [[understanding]{.underline}]{.mark} of the
**[[moral complexities]{.underline}]{.mark}** that are at the [[heart
of]{.mark} any social [policy]{.mark} issue]{.underline}.

Admittedly, [policy change]{.underline} is often
[[dependent]{.underline}]{.mark} on [[numerous political, social, and
economic factors]{.underline}]{.mark}. However, as John Kingdon has
argued else- where, [[politics and policy making]{.underline}]{.mark}
are driven by ideas as much as or even more than by [conventional
political processes]{.underline}.6 The fact remains that policies are
about choosing between a range of options and alternatives. Values,
which are at the [[heart of ethics]{.underline}]{.mark}, have been and
will continue to be instrumental in directing these choices. They allow
us to make [[reasoned]{.mark} arguments]{.underline} for why a
particular [[course of action]{.underline}]{.mark} is preferable over
another. When we consider what counts as a good reason in **[[policy
discourse]{.underline}]{.mark}** we are inevitably drawn into
[principles of [political]{.mark}]{.underline}
[[thought]{.underline}]{.mark}.7 All [public policies]{.underline}
therefore have implicit [normative dimensions]{.underline}. A
distinguishing feature of a care ethic is precisely that it is intended
to be an [[alternative source]{.mark} for [moral and political
judgments]{.mark}]{.underline}. It can be instrumental in contributing
to the kind of debate and discussion that often precipitate [fundamental
changes in social policy]{.underline}. If a care [[ethic and its
associated values]{.underline}]{.mark} came to bear on [[social
policy]{.mark} [decision making]{.mark}]{.underline}, then the
[[traditional **policy paradigm**]{.underline}]{.mark} would be replaced
by one that brings into view the realities of our lives and [aspects of
policy choices]{.underline} that have been traditionally
[[overlooked]{.underline}]{.mark} or ignored. A [[care
ethic]{.underline}]{.mark} brings to our attention the need to
[[contemplate]{.underline}]{.mark} how care fosters the necessary
[conditions]{.underline} for any [[society]{.underline}]{.mark} to
Xourish.

#### History proves\-\--ethics can transform societies

**McLellan '10** \[Betty; 2010; feminist ethicist and psychotherapist
with over 20 years' experience; Unspeakable : A Feminist Ethic of
Speech, P. 210-212; akshaj\]

The [[ultimate]{.mark} aim in the **[discipline of
ethics]{.mark}**]{.underline}, as stated in the Introduction, is to
[**[transform societies]{.mark}** with]{.underline} a view to
[[bringing]{.underline}]{.mark} about more [effective]{.underline} and
[[inclusive social justice systems]{.underline}]{.mark}. Thanks to the
[[constant activism]{.mark} of feminists in every
generation]{.underline}---analysing, researching, writing,
demonstrating, rallying, lobbying, speaking out---the lives of women in
Western countries have improved. Feminist historians and other
commentators acknowledge that there have been significant gains for
women in countries like the United States, Canada, Europe and Australia.

A [[huge step forward]{.underline}]{.mark} occurred at the end of the
nineteenth and the beginning of the twentieth century, when the battle
for [women's [suffrage was finally won in most **Western
countries**]{.mark}]{.underline}. For the first time, women were
permitted to vote on issues which affected their lives. The struggle
began in the mid-nineteenth century and continued for half a century
before the dreams of Elizabeth Cady Stanton, Lucretia Mott, Susan B.
Anthony, Emmeline Pankhurst and others were realised. Two world wars
later and married women had been manipulated from dependent wives
(before the wars) to independent workers (during the wars when they were
needed to fill the gaps in industry) and back to dependent wives (after
World War II). Then there began what Betty Friedan called a "strange
stirring" in women in the 1960s (1963, p. 13). Women started verbalising
dissatisfaction with the second-class status assigned to them by the
male hierarchy. **[[Consciousness-raising groups]{.underline}]{.mark}**
were formed in many local [communities [across]{.mark} the [Western
world]{.mark}]{.underline} and evidence of the extent of women's
oppression and the depth of their dissatisfaction came to light.In her
[history of the feminist movement]{.underline} in the United States, In
our Time: Memoir of a Revolution (1999), Susan Brownmiller begins by
discussing her own [experience of consciousness-raising
groups]{.underline} in the 1960s and moves on to discuss the
machinations inside the US movement as they fought for [women's rights
on many different fronts]{.underline}. Gains were made for [[individual
women in **equal opportunity**]{.underline}]{.mark}---education,
employment, politics, the military and business. In other areas, huge
battles were fought against rape, battery, sexual harassment and
pornography. While there were many gains along the way, US feminists
admit that there is still a long way to go.

Two significant battles which have taken [[**centre stage** for US
feminists over several decades have still not been
won]{.underline}]{.mark}. They are the Equal Rights Amendment to the
constitution and access to abortion. According to Brownmiller, "Women's
Liberation found its first unifying issue in abortion, and abortion
became the first feminist cause to sweep the nation", but she admits
that the fierce and sustained counterattack by opposing forces has meant
that "abortion remains our most important and pivotal issue, the
linchpin, then as now, of women's struggle for equality and reproductive
freedom" (1999, pp. 102, 135). Finishing her memoir on a positive note,
Brownmiller sums up her history of the feminist struggles in the United
States by saying:

Rarely in history have women been able to set aside their other
[[concerns and political causes]{.underline}]{.mark}, their divisions of
class, race, religion, ethnicity, their geographic boundaries and
personal attachments, in order to wage a united struggle, so
[[revolutionary in its implications]{.underline}]{.mark}, against their
basic, common oppression. Indeed, a full century passed between the
struggle for suffrage and what is often called the second wave. But when
such a coming-together takes place, when the vision is clear and the
**[[sisterhood is powerful]{.underline}]{.mark}**, mountains are moved
and the human landscape is changed forever \[p. 330\].

While Brownmiller presented a realistic picture of a movement which,
while making huge gains, also had some losses, Naomi Wolf in Fire with
Fire painted a positive picture of a battle won. She describes Second
Wave feminism in the United States as a "genderquake" and urges
feminists to stop focusing on the negatives and begin counting the
gains, calling on them to move into the future believing that women now
have equal power with men. Describing the genderquake, she says:

Twenty-five years of [[dedicated feminist]{.mark} activism have [hauled
the **political infrastructure**]{.mark} into place]{.underline}, enough
women in the middle classes have enough money and clout, and most women
now have enough [desire and [determination]{.mark}]{.underline} to begin
to [[balance the **imbalance of power**]{.underline}]{.mark} between the
sexes \[Wolf 1993, p. xvi\].

The problem, as she sees it, is with women themselves. She laments that
"women lack a [[psychology of female power]{.mark} to [match]{.mark}
their [new opportunities]{.mark}]{.underline}" (p. xvi).

Feminists working in the areas of rape, battery of women in the home and
the effects of prostitution and pornography, as well as those
[[studying]{.mark} the effects of the [deliberate
**subordination**]{.mark} and excl[usion of women from most areas of
power and influence]{.mark}]{.underline}, are critical of Wolf's call
for women to focus on the positive and ignore the negative. Feminists
have a choice, Wolf says: either to "understand that we are in the final
throes of a civil war for gender fairness in which conditions have
shifted to put much of the attainment of equality in women's own grasp"
or to continue "clinging to an [outdated image of ourselves as
powerless]{.underline}" (1993, pp. xv--xvi).

#### Ethic of care is the only way to establish interconnectedness

**Stephany '17** \[Kathleen; 03/25/2017; Faculty Health Sciences at
Douglas College; How to Help the Suicidal Person to Choose Life: The
Ethic of Care and Empathy as an Indispensable Tool for Intervention, P.
78; akshaj\]

In this current chapter, [specific [components]{.mark}]{.underline} of
the [[ethic of care and empathy]{.underline}]{.mark} are presented as an
actual tool for suicide intervention. These include,
[[establishing]{.mark} connection]{.underline}, fostering a [therapeutic
**[alliance]{.mark}**]{.underline}, offering [[unconditional
positive]{.mark} [regard]{.mark}]{.underline}, heartfelt listening,
presencing and compassion. Key aspects of The Aeschi Working Group of
suicidologists' Guidelines for Clinicians will be reviewed. A touching
narrative case study is presented where we learn how a total stranger
helped a suicidal girl through an act of compassion. A psychological
autopsy follows this story and assists us in gaining a retrospective
view of what went wrong in the emergency room (ER) and why. Near the end
of the Chapter, **[[Cognitive Therapy]{.underline}]{.mark}** is
recommended once a suicidal person's initial crisis has subsided. A
[[dynamic simulation]{.underline}]{.mark} exercise is then presented in
two parts to help gatekeepers practice being empathetic with a suicidal
patient. In closing, caregivers are encouraged to [[practice
empathy]{.underline}]{.mark} in their daily round through the act of
journaling to increase self-awareness.

[THE ETHIC OF CARE AS THE WEB OF CONNECTION]{.underline}

The [[ethic of care]{.underline}]{.mark} is [[**multi-dimensional** and
values]{.mark} our [relationships to others]{.mark}]{.underline},
context, a [[sense of]{.underline} [community]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[and]{.underline}]{.mark} our
**[[interconnectedness]{.underline}]{.mark}** (Gilligan, 1982; Noddings;
1984; Watson, 2008). The [ethic of care]{.underline} also
[[admonishes]{.underline}]{.mark} us to attend to the [[needs of]{.mark}
all other [persons]{.mark}]{.underline}, including those in our [[direct
care]{.underline}]{.mark} as well as everyone else, even those we do not
know (Noddings, 1984; Slote, 2007). In fact, caring for one another is
not [depicted as a nicety]{.underline}, it is [[portrayed as a necessity
for the **survival of all humans**]{.underline}]{.mark}. For example, if
we do not care about everyone we will not take [moral
action]{.underline} to help those who are in need, or to [preserve
life]{.underline} (Noddings, 1984; Slote, 2007). In fact the [[ethic of
care **promotes** a web of connection]{.underline}]{.mark} that is
[[inclusive of everyone and excludes no one]{.underline}]{.mark}
(Gilligan, 1982; Stephany, 2012).

### Alt\-\--International Movements

#### Forefront feminist pedagogy is key to trans-national feminist movements\-\--only starting from people's experience of the state can contribute to change. 

Rebecca **Sanders 20**. Associate Professor in the Department of
Political Science and an affiliate faculty member in the Department of
Women\'s, Gender, and Sexuality Studies at the University of Cincinnati.
Review: Revisiting gendered states: feminist imaginings of the state in
international relations. International Affairs. 03-01-2020. 96(2)

As with all edited volumes, what is gained in diversity of cases is
occasionally lost in overall analytical consistency. The contributions
work well together, but function more as independent chapters than
integral parts of a central argument. Moreover, while providing broad
insight into gendered states, questions remain about the implications of
these observations for emancipatory feminist goals. For instance,
Parashar, Tickner and True challenge depictions of the gender-neutral
state, but leave open the possibility of the state as source of gender
justice (p. 3). Yet the chapters do not coalesce around a distinctive
prescription for overcoming the forms of oppression and inequality that
are so often constructed and reinforced through gendered states.
*Tickner draws inspiration from* **[local and international [indigenous
struggles]{.mark}]{.underline}** *for justice, highlighting the
significance of pursuing change [through]{.mark} multilevel*
**[[pluralistic governance]{.underline}]{.mark}** (pp. 29--30). *True
hopes [transnational feminist movements]{.mark} [can pressure gendered
states]{.mark}* to respond to reactionary backlash against women's
rights by becoming more equitable and inclusive (pp. 46--7). David
Duriesmith argues that *liberal states can become* **[feminist
allies]{.underline}**, *but only if they systematically acknowledge and
dismantle male supremacy* (pp. 65--6). Lesley Pruitt suggests that
*[countries in the global South]{.mark} can make unique contributions to
expanding and* **[[implementing the Women, Peace, and Security
agenda]{.mark} in innovative ways]{.underline}** (pp. 128--9) *that
transcend neo-liberal visions of gender equality that have become
normative in the global North* (pp. 136--7). Katrina Lee-Koo cautions
that **[tolerance of 'pragmatic patriarchy' during crises locks in
gender inequality, making it difficult to challenge in the long
term]{.underline}** (p. 139). In sum, Revisiting gendered states
provides rich new material that will be of interest to feminist IR
scholars and students studying the role of gender in international and
comparative politics. The chapters are highly amenable to being assigned
on syllabuses. Researchers considering new projects will find
inspiration in the multiplicity of cases and themes identified in the
volume. At the same time, these qualities mean that those seeking
definitive answers to how we might transcend gendered states may be left
with as many questions as answers. In the face of these instabilities
and ambiguities, Christine Sylvester's concluding admonishment that *w[e
would be wise to 'study]{.mark} the state as* **[[people's experiences
of the state]{.underline}]{.mark}***,* **[[not so much the state
itself]{.underline}]{.mark}** ' (p. 195) *helpfully [reminds us of
the]{.mark} unique, [bottom-up]{.mark}, humanistic contribution that
[feminist perspectives]{.mark} can offer to the understanding of
political life*.

#### Solves the case\-\--

#### Drawing on the imaginaries of feminists such as [Elise Boulding]{.underline} and [Rokeya Hossein]{.underline} creates futrues without [weapons]{.underline} or [sovereignty]{.underline}\-\--it removes the impetus for [defense pacts]{.underline}.

Minna **Lyytikäinen 20**. Doctoral Candidate in Political Science at the
University of Helsinki. Punam Yadav, Annick TR Wibben, Marjaana
Jauhola, Catia Cecilia Confortini. Unruly wives in the household: Toward
feminist genealogies for peace research. Cooperation and Conflict.
07-07-2020.
https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/0010836720938397

This led her to begin *[her]{.mark} own [work]{.mark} on
[imagining]{.mark}* **[alternative, peaceful
[futures]{.mark}]{.underline}** by holding workshops where participants
were projecting 30 years into the future to ask: "**[[If there are no
more weapons]{.underline}]{.mark}***, how is the world functioning*?"
(Boulding, 1988, 1989). Revisiting this work at the end of the 1980s,
Boulding wrote, "I am not exactly sure when it began to dawn on me that
most of the peace movement activists I knew, from armscontrollers to
out-and-out disarmers, did not in their hearts believe that a world
without armies was possible" (1989: 74). Meanwhile, due to her work on
utopian imagery in varied civilizational traditions, *Boulding realized
that "[an inclination to visualize]{.mark} one's own society in [a
future peaceable state]{.mark} was testified to in the literary and oral
traditions of* **[every major culture]{.underline}**" (1989: 74), yet
*[this was]{.mark} almost completely [lacking in "the privileged
sectors]{.mark} of the* **[industrialized world]{.underline}**, both
East and West" (1989: 74). At the same time, it was clear to her "that
the human capacity for imagining the good society is not lost, only
weakened. It can be nurtured back to vigorousness, liveliness" (1989:
81).

*[A key element]{.mark} of Boulding's research to strengthen her
understanding of* **[what a weapons-free society]{.underline}** *would
look like* (and teaching it to others) [*was the* **[knowledge she
accumulated]{.underline}**]{.mark} *of peaceful societies and everyday
behaviors* (e.g. Boulding, 1992b, 1996, 2000). **[Always a
scholar-activist]{.underline}**, in her key works---The Underside of
History: A View of Women Through Time (1992b) and Cultures of Peace: The
Hidden History (2000)---"*she makes a cogent plea for educators to get
to know alternative histories*, and for engendering some practical hope"
(Hutchinson and Milojevic, 2012: 152). In Cultures of Peace, Boulding
specifically emphasized that the "*[recovery of]{.mark} hidden strengths
of [local cultures]{.mark} is one important aspect of peace building*
for this painful transitional period in contemporary history" (2000:
92). After reviewing the practices of several contemporary communities,
she proposed: "underneath the layers of violence each society, without
exception has its peace behaviors, *precious resources [that can be
available to bring about]{.mark} new and [gentler forms of
governance]{.mark} locally and [on a larger scale]{.mark} in the next
century*" (2000: 101).

### Alt\-\--Ontological Revisionism 

#### Vote negative for ontological revisionism\-\--shifting away from androcentric biases

**Youngs '04** \[Gillian; 01/2004; Senior Lecturer at the University of
Leicester; Feminist International Relations: A Contradiction in Terms?
Or: Why Women and Gender Are Essential to Understanding the World \'We\'
Live in, p. 77-80; akshaj\]

**[Ontological revisionism]{.underline}**

This discussion will demonstrate, in the ways outlined above, the [depth
and range of [feminist perspectives on power]{.mark}-a prime concern of
International Relations]{.underline} and indeed of the whole study of
politics. It will illustrate the varied ways in which scholars using
these [[perspectives study power in relation to
gender]{.underline}]{.mark}, a nexus largely disregarded in mainstream
approaches. From [feminist positions]{.underline}, this lacuna marks out
[**[mainstream]{.mark}** analyses as [trapped in]{.mark} a narrow and
**[superficial]{.mark}** [ontological and epistemological
framework]{.mark}]{.underline}. A major part of the problem is the way
in which the [mainstream]{.underline} takes the appearance of a [[pre-
dominantly **male-constructed** reality]{.underline}]{.mark} as a given,
and thus as the beginning and end of investigation and
knowledge-building. [[Feminism requires an ontological
revisionism]{.underline}]{.mark}: a recognition that it is necessary to
go behind the appearance and examine how differentiated and
[[gendered]{.mark} power [constructs]{.mark} the social relations [that
form that]{.mark} [reality]{.mark}]{.underline}.

While it may be empirically accurate to observe that historically and
contemporaneously [[men]{.mark} have **[dominated]{.mark}** the realms
of [international politics and economics]{.mark}]{.underline},
[feminists argue]{.underline} that a [full]{.underline} understanding of
the nature of those [[realms must]{.mark} [include]{.mark} understanding
the [intricate patterns of **(gendered) inequalities** that shape
them]{.mark}]{.underline}. Mainstream International Relations, in
[accepting]{.underline} that because these [realms]{.underline} appear
to be [predominantly man-made]{.underline}, there is no reason to ask
how or why that is the case, [stop short of taking account of
gender]{.underline}. As long as those who adhere to this position
continue [to accept the sufficiency of the appearances and [probe no
further]{.mark}]{.underline}, then the [[ontological and epistemological
**limitations** will continue to be reproduced]{.underline}]{.mark}.

Early work in feminist International Relations in the I980s had to
address this problem directly by [[peeling back]{.mark} the
[masculinist]{.mark} surface of world [politics]{.mark} to
[reveal]{.mark} its more [complex gendered]{.mark} (and racialized)
**[dynamics]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Key scholars such as Cynthia Enloe
focused on core International Relations issues of war, militarism and
security, highlighting the [[dependence of]{.mark} these concepts on
[gender structures]{.mark}]{.underline}-e.g. [dominant forms of the
[masculine]{.mark}]{.underline} (warrior) [**[subject]{.mark}** as
protector/conqueror/exploiter]{.underline} of the
[[feminine]{.mark}/feminized **[object]{.mark}**/other-and thus the
[fundamental]{.mark} [importance]{.mark} of [subjecting them to **gender
analysis**]{.mark}]{.underline}. In a series of works, including the
early Bananas, beaches and bases: making [feminist sense of
international politics]{.underline} (I989), Enloe has addressed
di[fferent [aspects]{.mark} of the most [overtly]{.mark}
[masculine]{.mark} realms of international relations]{.underline},
conflict and defence, to reveal their [deeper [gendered
realities]{.mark}]{.underline}.3 This body of work has launched a
**[[powerful critique]{.underline}]{.mark}** of the taboo that [made
[women]{.mark} and gender most [invisible]{.mark}]{.underline}, in
theory and practice, where [[masculinity]{.underline}]{.mark} had its
[most extreme]{.underline}, defining ([and
**[violent]{.mark}**]{.underline}) expression. Enloe\'s research has
provided one of the most comprehensive bodies of evidence for the
[[ontological revisionism required]{.underline}]{.mark} of mainstream
[International Relations]{.underline}, [especially in relation to its
core concerns]{.underline}.

When Enloe claimed that \'gender makes the world go round\',4 fact
turning the [[abstract logic]{.mark} of malestream International
Relations]{.underline} This abstract logic saw little need to take
[theoretical and [analytic gender as a **social
force**]{.mark}]{.underline} because in [practical terms only one
gender]{.underline} appeared to define International Relations. Ann
Tickner has recently the reminder that this situation persists: \'During
the I990s, women w to most combat positions in the U.S. military, and
the U.S. president appointed the first female secretary of state, but
occupations in foreign and military [**[policy-making]{.mark}** in most
states remain [overwhelmingly]{.mark} male, and usually **[elite
male]{.mark}**]{.underline}.\'5

Nearly a decade earlier, in her groundbreaking work Gender in
International Relations: [[feminist perspectives on achieving **global
security**]{.underline}]{.mark},6 she had asked the kinds of
[[questions]{.underline}]{.mark} that were [[foundational]{.mark} to
early feminist [I]{.mark}nternational [R]{.mark}elations]{.underline}:
\'Why is the subject matter of my discipline so distant from women\'s
lived experiences? Why have [[women]{.underline}]{.mark} been
conspicuous only by their [[absence]{.mark} in the worlds [of
diplomacy]{.mark} and military and foreign policy-making]{.underline}?\'
Tickner, like Enloe, has [interrogated]{.underline} core issues in
mainstream International Relations, such as [security and
peace]{.underline}, providing [[feminist bases for]{.mark} gendered
[understanding of issues]{.mark}]{.underline} that have defined it. Her
[reflection]{.underline} on what has happened since Gender in
International Relations was published indicates the [[prominence of
tensions between theory and practice]{.underline}]{.mark}. \'We may have
provided some answers to my questions as to why [IR and foreign
policymaking [remain male-dominated]{.mark}]{.underline}; but breaking
down the [[unequal]{.mark} gender [hierarchies]{.mark} that
[perpetuate]{.mark} these **[androcentric biases]{.mark}**]{.underline}
remains a challenge.\'7

The [[persistence of]{.mark}]{.underline} the overriding [[maleness of
i]{.mark}nternational [r]{.mark}elations]{.underline} in practice is
part of the reason for the [continued resistance and [lack of
responsiveness]{.mark} to the analytical relevance feminist
International Relations claims]{.underline}. In other words, it is to
some extent not surprising that feminist International Relations stands
largely [outside mainstream International Relations]{.underline},
[because]{.underline} the [concerns of the former]{.underline}, gender
and women, continue to appear to be [subsidiary to high politics and
diplomacy]{.underline}. One has only to recall the limited attention to
gender and women in the recent Afghanistan and Iraq crises to illustrate
this point.8 So how have feminists tackled this problem? Necessarily,

**ontological revisionism**]{.mark}]{.underline}. I say problematically
because, bearing in mind the limited success of the first kind discussed
above, it can be anticipated that this deeper kind is likely to be even
more challenging for those in the mainstream camp.

The second level of [[ontological revisionism required]{.mark} relates
[to **critical understanding**]{.mark}]{.underline} of why the

configured as ([feminized]{.underline}) [\'others\' because of racial,
colonial and other factors]{.underline}, including sexuality. Marysia

[[dominance of the male]{.mark} subject position in the realm of

The [[project\'s]{.underline}]{.mark} starting point [revealed a [faith
in the **modernist commitment**]{.mark} to the political importance of
[bringing women into the position of
**subjecthood**]{.mark}]{.underline}. We implicitly accepted that
women\'s subjecthood could be exposed and revealed in the study and
practice of international relations, hoping that this would also
[[reveal the nature of male dominance]{.mark} and power]{.underline}.
Posing the \'man\' question instead reflects our [diminishing

can be [remedied by [converting them into
subjects]{.mark}]{.underline}.\"

[Adding women]{.underline} appeared to have failed to
[**[\'destabilize\']{.mark}** the [field]{.mark}]{.underline}; so
perhaps [critically [addressing]{.mark}]{.underline} its [prime subject
\'man\' head-on]{.underline} could help to do so. \'This leads us to ask
questions about the [[roles of masculinity]{.mark} in]{.underline} the
conduct of [international relations]{.underline} and to
[question]{.underline} the [[accepted
**naturalness**]{.underline}]{.mark} of the abundance of men in the
theory and practice of international relations\' (emphasis added).I2 The
[[deeper level of ontological revisionism]{.underline}]{.mark} called
for by feminist Inter- national Relations in this regard is as follows.
Not only does it press beyond the appearance of [international
relations]{.underline} as a [[predominantly]{.underline}]{.mark}
[**[masculine terrain]{.mark}** by [including women in its
analysis]{.mark}]{.underline}, it goes further to question the
predominant masculinity itself and the accepted naturalness of its power
and [[influence]{.mark} in [collective]{.mark} (most significantly
[state) and individual forms]{.mark}]{.underline}.

### AT: Microaggressions

#### Dualist understanding solves\-\--increases positive feelings, compassion, and devotion

**Penttinen '13** \[Elina; 03/11/2013; University Lecturer in
International Relations at the University of Tampere, Finland; Joy and
International Relations: A New Methodology, P. 68-69; akshaj\]

[Scientific [research on **mindfulness**]{.mark} training]{.underline}
and practice abounds in [clinical and health studies]{.underline},
[neurosciences and neurophenomenology]{.underline} (Kabat-Zinn 2003,
2005).3 These studies shift the [**[dualist understanding]{.mark}**
between thought and non- thought]{.underline}, showing how meditation,
the [[practice of cultivating positive emotions]{.underline}]{.mark},
has [[concrete]{.mark} measurable **[consequences]{.mark}** on the
body]{.underline}. For example, studies show that meditation builds the
immune system, and [[facilitates]{.mark} and speeds
[healing]{.mark}]{.underline} alongside Western medicine. There is also
a [[growing]{.mark} body of [research on
alternative]{.mark}]{.underline} healing methods (Franquemont 2009) and
[scientific studies of prayer for healing]{.underline} (Dossey 2010),
which all point to the [need to [develop **conceptual
knowledge**]{.mark}]{.underline} of the mind-body interaction, and
[[understand nonlinear time and nonlocal
communication]{.underline}]{.mark} in the [context of health and
healing]{.underline}.

In addition to [clinical research in health sciences and
psychology]{.underline} there has been [[considerable interest]{.mark}
[in]{.mark} the cognitive and **[neurosciences]{.mark}**]{.underline} in
investigating the effect [of [meditation on brain
activity]{.mark}]{.underline} (Kabat-Zinn 2003; Hölzel et al. 2011).
There have been [cross-cultural research projects]{.underline} involving
Tibetan monks practicing meditations and neuroscientists measuring brain
activity during meditation. Understanding the [[Eastern]{.mark}
philosophical [tradition]{.mark}]{.underline} of meditation is
[[**essential** for]{.mark} interpreting the [data]{.mark}]{.underline}.
The investigation of specific meditative areas, such as [[positive
feelings]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[compassion]{.underline}]{.mark} and the
[[devotion]{.mark} of a trained long-term monastic
practitioner]{.underline} "show a range of [[**stable patterns** of

[[replicated by the subject]{.mark} at will]{.underline}, depending on

[[research projects]{.underline}]{.mark} show that [[mindfulness
meditation]{.underline}]{.mark} concretely [[changes brain]{.mark}
activity and capacity]{.underline}. It is also shown to increase the

projects [complement contemporary [research on brain
plasticity]{.mark}]{.underline} in [[repairing]{.mark} **[neurological
damage]{.mark}**]{.underline}. The evidence of these research projects
[moves [beyond]{.mark} the **[mechanistic paradigm]{.mark}** and
computational [model of the human brain]{.mark}]{.underline}4 and

I would like to share [my personal experience [as an **independent
Parliament member**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [an architect]{.underline},
[and a Syrian woman]{.underline}. I decided to run for Parliament during
a difficult period of the Syrian crisis because I believed in defending
my right and the right of the Syrian people to have freedom of
expression and the freedom to determine our destiny. This right has been
denied by some countries that claim to speak on behalf of our people
without considering the realities on the ground. These countries claim
to champion human rights and to support the Syrian people and their
freedoms. Instead, they have violated the sovereignty of the Syrian
state by manufacturing an external opposition of people who claim to be
the only legitimate representative of the Syrian public despite not
having lived in Syria for over twenty years. This opposition allegedly
wants to overthrow the current secular regime in order to institute
democracy. However, [[**freedom** and **democracy** cannot be achieved
through]{.mark} arming civilians]{.underline}, [using
**[intimidation]{.mark} tactics**]{.underline}, [dismissing and
[**disregarding** the **majority**]{.mark}'s opinion]{.underline},
[opening borders to global networks of
**[terrorists]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [spreading
**[sectarianism]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [disseminating **false
[propaganda]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [or changing facts]{.underline} on
the ground.

As a patriotic Syrian Christian woman, I felt a strong responsibility to
mitigate the crisis in my country, as did many other Syrian women, who
have shown great resolve and endurance. Like many[, [I experienced the
**widespread corruption** in Syria]{.underline}, [but I
refused]{.underline}]{.mark} to let my emotions determine my reaction to
these conditions, as others have, or [[to ignite a war in my
country]{.underline}]{.mark}. Recognizing that the crisis is
quintessentially a national issue, I chose the Syrian Parliament as a
legitimate platform upon which to advocate for reform, defend the rights
of my people, and work for peace and true democracy in my country.

Through this international forum that advocates for democracy worldwide,
I stand today, along with every parliamentarian who was elected by the
public, to represent the Syrian people. Although the Syrian public did
not elect the opposition, Syria's doors are open to the internal and
external opposition groups if they wish to participate in its
governance. However, they must be elected if they wish to represent the
Syrian people[. [Those who want to **change** the
**system**]{.mark}]{.underline}, or even the regime, [**[must
provide]{.underline}** [a **clear** and **legitimate**
proposal]{.underline}]{.mark} [for reform [that can be supported at the
ballot]{.mark} and ensure that it does not advance foreign
agendas]{.underline}.

[[Legitimacy **cannot be won** through]{.mark} war, killing innocent
people, or [destroying the **state's infrastructure** under the pretext
of changing the regime]{.mark} or protecting civilians]{.underline}. As
a woman parliamentarian, I join you in assuming the responsibility for
protecting people's freedom and women's roles in politics and society. I
invite you to stand with the Syrian people in order to stop the violence
and work for peace in Syria and the Middle East. I reiterate our
rejection of any international intervention that ignores our role as
legitimately elected women parliamentarians, or that intervenes in our
affairs or the sovereignty of any other state.

#### [BAHRANI PROTESTS]{.underline}. They worked through the state and got the right to vote for women.

Political Context of Bahrain

[["\[Bahraini women\] have been **shoulder-to-shoulder** with men
fighting the \[British\] occupation in the 1950s and
1960s]{.underline},]{.mark}" remarked a Bahraini activist and journalist
(personal interview, November 26, 2014, Houston, Texas). The period of
the 1950s witnessed the birth of a unique moment in Bahrain's history,
with the rise of a nonsectarian political movement challenging the
ruling power of Sheikh Salman bin Hamad al-Khalifa and his British
advisor (Coates Ulrichsen 2013). Mounting discontent concerning British
policies in Bahrain and the sweeping Arab nationalism served as potent
catalysts to mobilize Bahrainis for a unified cause and eventually to
seek Bahrain's independence from Britain in 1971.

[[The newly formed government set]{.underline}]{.mark} in place a
constitution in 1973 that guaranteed [**[universal
suffrage]{.underline}**, [though it denied women the right to
vote]{.underline}]{.mark}. Women were viewed as a means to strengthen
the nation, and their education and participation in the country's
development were considered necessary elements for a modern state
following independence (Al Gharaibeh 2011, 96). From independence until
the turn of the century, however, women achieved minor advances in the
realm of formal politics, whereas women's education, economic
capability, and organization flourished (Pandya 2012; Seikaly 1994).

Between 1994 and 1999, political and social unrest rocked Bahrain once
again with the Uprising of Dignity, in which both liberals and Islamists
joined forces to demand employment opportunities, social justice,
democratic reforms, and a stronger role for the Parliament (Coates
Ulrichsen 2013; Seikaly 1997). Central to these demands were the
political rights and participation of women (Al Gharaibeh 2011, 97).
Indeed[, [women themselves **mobilized to fight** for their rights and
started a **petition** in 1995]{.underline}]{.mark} that articulated
their concerns about the state of Bahraini society and their desire for
concrete reforms that would include women in the political process
(Seikaly 1997, 125).

[[Protests erupted once again in 200]{.underline}1]{.mark}. In response,
a National Action Charter was proposed as an attempt to appease opposing
voices Women's Political Participation in Bahrain
[[and]{.underline}]{.mark} make the government more responsive to
citizens and inclusive of women. Remarkably, it was the precursor to a
new constitution in 2002, which called for the establishment of
elections for [[the Parliament]{.underline}]{.mark} and [[gave women the
right to vote and **participate** in public affai]{.mark}r]{.underline}s
(Fadhl 2008). "The Bahraini women were participating in protests,
participating in life," remarked a member of the Gulf Center for Human
Rights (personal interview, December 3, 2014. Houston, Texas). A scholar
researching Bahrain at the time of the first elections reflected on that
historic moment, saying, "They were so excited to be able to actually
participate. It was the participation that got women charged up. And
that moved on to activism in other areas" (personal interview, December
3, 2014, Houston, Texas). While the National Action Charter and
universal suffrage opened doors in the formal political realm, women
continued to face challenges that curtailed their advancement.

### Perm - Progress Possible

#### Progress is [possible]{.underline} through the state. 

**Ferree 21 \[Myra Marx; Alice H. Cook Professor of Sociology Emerita,

Center for European Studies, Harvard University; "Commentary on Women
Rising: In and Beyond the Arab Spring," April 7, 2021,
https://nyupress.org/blog/2021/04/07/commentary-on-women-rising-in-and-beyond-the-arab-spring/?,
Accessed June 26th, 2025.\] rishabh**

A third theme is that of active citizenship, of being part of a nation
that is struggling to find its way to make a state that will be free of
the autocracy, violence, oppressions and exclusions that their nations
have been experiencing. These voices say that openings for democracy are
pregnant with possibility for women, but these voices also tell of
dreadful abuses and both institutional violence and impunity. [[The
desire to be citizens with **full rights** to participation]{.underline}
[in determining the future of the state]{.underline}]{.mark} that will
govern them [[is articulated in]{.underline}]{.mark} so many different
forms, from the claim to [[legislative
representation]{.underline}]{.mark} in it to the resistance to violence
in the movements to overthrow it completely. Despite the downward
spirals into violence that have torn Syria and Iraq apart with such

respectful of difference and nonviolent in practice remains a commitment

[[There is]{.mark}]{.underline} finally also [[a theme of
hope]{.underline}]{.mark}. By looking back on slow change and frequent
repression in the past, the stories here also [[point forward with hope
rather than resignation or despair]{.underline}]{.mark}. Whether the
account is of painfully slow reform processes in Saudi Arabia, or of the
destructive choice of the Syrian resistance to take up armed resistance,
or the civil war erupting in Yemen, the tragic implications are also
illuminated by the emphasis on how it could be otherwise someday.
Women's efforts to make reform are continuing even when the situation is
deteriorating. Hope is flower that appears in the form of graffiti
saying "no" to Syrian repression and to revolutionary violence.

The Arab Spring of 2011 is a decade in the past already, and many of the
authors are looking back from a place that they never anticipated being
in when they engaged in social movements in this period. The stories
offer not only accounts of the surprises that sometimes came next, for
example, in the form of repressive dictatorship in Egypt and wars in
Syria and Yemen, but also the ongoing developments in which the spring
of 2011 was merely a minor punctuation mark in a struggle for democracy
that began decades or more before and which is still being pursued
today. The results of these less glamourous transformations, as with
changes in the personal code that governs family relations, draw less
Western attention but are shown here as part of a diversity of changes
across the MENA region. Just as there was not one Arab Spring even
though revolutionary energies flowed across borders in the region a
decade ago, there is not one Arab winter now either, [[as women's
demands for **individual freedoms**, collective **recognition**,
democratic **influence** and a non-violent future]{.mark} also
[circulate]{.mark} and sustain [hope]{.mark}.]{.underline}

#### **Women are [more likely]{.underline} to have food insecurity, water shortages, and be [structurally]{.underline} behind with warming.**

Amongst the vulnerable populations, [[women are **severely impacted** by
climatic change]{.underline}]{.mark} and variations -- [rural
women]{.underline} who are mostly reliant on agriculture [are severely
affected]{.underline} (Brown et al. 2012). Rural women consider
agriculture their only security to sustain their poverty-stricken
livelihoods, families, and communities. In countries such as Bangladesh,
[[vulnerability to]{.mark} the devastating effects of [climate
change]{.mark}]{.underline} and income inequalities [[have **increased
food insecurity**]{.underline}]{.mark} (FAO 2015; Tanjeela and
Rutherford 2018). In Bangladesh, the climate victims are mostly women
who are poverty stricken. These [[women are more vulnerable than
men]{.mark} in both [rural and urban
situations]{.mark}]{.underline}[,]{.mark} which are [[exacerbated by
**gender inequality**]{.underline}]{.mark}, and food insecurity has
shaped vulnerability and resilience amongst communities prone to
climate-induced disasters (Shamsuddoha 2012; Tanjeela and Rutherford
2018). According to Cutter (2017) and Watts et al. (2018), [[food
hierarchies]{.mark} have a likelihood to [make
women]{.mark}]{.underline} (possible when pregnant or breastfeeding)
[[and children susceptible to]{.mark} the increased **risks of
[malnutrition]{.mark}** [and water shortages]{.mark}]{.underline} during
and after disasters. Similarly, in the aftermath of disasters, women,
children, the elderly, and people with disabilities fnd it difcult to
bounce back because of income inequality and fnancial dependence. They
are usually unable to independently make decisions to migrate or stay in
areas which are struck by disasters (Jordan 2018; Ayeb-Karlsson et al.
2020).

According to Zaidi and Fordham (2021), the risks and vulnerabilities of
women due to disasters are perpetual in nature as they supersede various
interventions (including Fig. 1 Frequently occurring words. Sams (2019:
6) argues that in the [[patriarchal society]{.mark} of Bangladesh
amongst the climate migrants]{.underline}, the unequal socio-economic
conditions [[have **condemned women to poverty**]{.underline}]{.mark},
limited access to and control over natural resources, [[and a lower
status]{.mark} in the family and society]{.underline}, therefore
[[increasing]{.mark} their [vulnerability to
discrimination]{.mark}]{.underline}. Ayeb-Karlsson et al. (2020) opines
that when a disaster strikes, women's mobility tend to be at risk and
they are usually constrained to their homes as compared to their male
(masculine) counterparts who have the ability and fexibility to move.
Rhodes et al. (2010) opine that patriarchal power and socio-cultural
systems as well as the socio-political processes exacerbate gender
imbalances during and post disasters. Gender power relations and
imbalances are also experienced during capacity development regarding
disaster preparedness training (including early warning systems),
shelter for the displaced and social positions (Nadiruzzaman and
Wrathall 2015). In countries like Pakistan, the perceived norms, roles,
and responsibilities of women have generally placed them at a
disadvantaged position which make them vulnerable to climate induced
gender-based violence (Memon 2020).

Climate change and extreme weather events have laid bare major gender
inequalities as the marginalised sub-groups have minimal capacity to
adapt to climate change variations (Benjamin and Thomas 2020; Memon
2020) with excessive harassment recorded against women in Northern
Bangladesh (Masson et al. 2019). Gender disproportions have been notable
within rural populace as women are seen as more vulnerable to climate
change. This situation is a threat to human security and can have
devastating implications for agro-based livelihoods (Chidakwa et al.
2020). In Africa, countries such as Zimbabwe have experienced the
devastating impacts of climate change as women, children and the elderly
rely on rain-fed agriculture and are deeply vulnerable when their
agricultural production is drastically reduced because of urbanisation
and the emigration of their men to cities and neighbouring countries
(Chidakwa et al. 2020). A susceptibility to the efects of natural
disasters is also experienced by seasonal farmworkers and socially
isolated migrants and low-income people (Burke et al. 2012) with women
experiencing more violence than their male counterparts (Camey et al.
2020). Meanwhile, [[women are]{.mark} disproportionately [susceptible to
climate-induced disasters]{.mark} [especially]{.mark} those [in the
informal sector]{.mark} who are mostly seen as providing support to
their families and communities]{.underline} (Datey et al. 2023).

### War Turns K\-\--2AC

[[Afghanistan]{.underline}]{.mark}.
